brushes and slip rings for the revolving field alternator (see the section called “Brushless Exciters 
with Solid-State Voltage Control”).
ROTATING-FIELD ALTERNATORS
Rotating-field alternators are used extensively because of the ease with which a high-voltage 
or high-current load can be connected to the alternator. The load connections do not need 
to draw power from the armature through brushes riding on slip rings. In this alternator, 
the output power is taken from the stationary armature through solid electrical connections. 
The armature is the stationary component of the generator, and the DC field is connected to 
the rotating field. The field windings in large generators are typically smaller than the armature 
windings. Therefore, the field has less mass and weight to rotate and can be supplied with a 
relatively small amount of DC to generate a relatively large amount of AC in the armature.
FIELD EXCITATION
DC must be used in the electromagnetic field circuit of an alternator. As a result, all types of 
alternators must be supplied with field current from a DC source, except for small perma-
nent magnet fields. The DC source may be a DC generator operated on the same shaft as 
the alternator. In this case, the DC generator is called an exciter, shown on the self-excited 
synchronous alternator in Figure 11–4(A). The circuit diagram for this alternator is shown in 
Figure 11–4(B). In installations where a number of alternators require excitation power, this 
power is supplied by a DC generator driven by a separate prime mover. The output terminals of 
this generator connect to a DC exciter bus from which other alternators receive their excitation 
power by means of brushes and slip rings for the rotating-field type of alternator.

FIELD DISCHARGE CIRCUIT
A field discharge switch is used in the excitation circuit of an alternator. This switch eliminates 
the potential danger to personnel and equipment resulting from the high inductive voltage cre-
ated when the DC field circuit is opened.
Figure 11–5 illustrates the connections for the field circuit of a separately excited alter-
nator. With the discharge switch closed, the field circuit is energized, and the field discharge 
switch functions as a normal double-pole, single-throw switch.
FIGURE 11–5 Field discharge switch.
© Cengage Learning 2014
The discharge switch shown in Figure 11–5 has an auxiliary switch blade at A in addition 
to the normal blades at C and D (Figure 11–5).
When you want to open the field circuit, the following actions must take place.
• Before the main switch contacts open, switch blade A meets contact B and thus provides 
a second path for the current through the field discharge resistor.
• When the main switch contacts C-D open (shown in Figure 11–6), high inductive volt-
age is created in the field coils by the collapsing magnetic field.
• The high voltage is dissipated by sending a current through the field discharge resistor.

This procedure eliminates the possibility of damage to the insulation of the field windings

FREQUENCY
The frequency of an alternator is a direct function of (a) the speed of rotation of the armature 
or the field and (b) the number of poles in the field circuit. The frequency commonly used 
in the United States is 60 cycles per second, or 60 Hz. Power companies are particularly con-
cerned with maintaining a constant frequency for their energy output because many devices 
depend on a constant value of frequency. This constant value is achieved by sensitive control 
of the prime mover speed, driving the alternator.
If the number of field poles in a given alternator is known, then it is possible to determine 
the speed required to produce a desired frequency. One cycle of voltage is generated each 
time an armature conductor passes across two field poles of opposite magnetic polarity. The 
frequency in cycles per second, or hertz, is the number of pairs of poles passed by the conduc-
tor in a second. Because the speed of rotating machinery is given in revolutions per minute 
(r/min), the speed in revolutions per second is obtained by dividing the speed (r/min) by 60. 
In a two-pole alternator the frequency is

VOLTAGE CONTROL
The voltage output of an alternator increases as the speed of rotation accelerates, thus increas-
ing the lines of force cut per second. If the field excitation increases, the magnetic fields are 
increased to the point of magnetic saturation of the field poles. Increasing the magnetic field 
strength increases output voltage.
For practical purposes, an alternator must be operated at a constant speed to maintain a 
fixed frequency. Thus, the only feasible method of controlling the voltage output is to vary the 
field excitation.
Field controls are used to vary the resistance of the total field circuit. This variation of 
resistance, in turn, changes the value of field current [Figure 11–4(B)].
• A low value of field current results in less flux and less induced voltage at a given speed.
• A high field current results in greater field flux and a higher induced voltage at a given speed.
• The value of flux at which the field poles saturate determines the maximum voltage 
obtainable at a fixed speed and frequency.
STATOR WINDINGS
Figure 11–7 illustrates the stator (stationary or nonmoving) windings of a rotating-field, three-
phase alternator. The three-phase armature windings are embedded 120° from one another 
in the slots of a laminated steel core that is clamped securely to the alternator frame. Output 
leads from the stator emerge from the bottom of the stator and connect directly to the load 
circuit. Slip rings and brushes are not required in a stationary winding of this type. As a result, 
higher values of output voltage and current are possible. Standard values of voltage output for 
a rotating-field alternator are as high as 13,800 volts.

ROTATING FIELD
The rotating portion of a rotating-field alternator consists of field poles mounted on a shaft 
that is driven by the prime mover. The magnetic flux established by the rotating field poles cuts 
across the conductors of the stator winding to produce the induced output voltage of the stator.
The following comparison can be made between the rotating-armature alternator and 
the rotating-field alternator. In the rotating-armature alternator, the armature conductors cut 
the flux established by stationary field poles. For the rotating-field alternator, the motionless 
conductors of the stator winding are cut by the flux established by rotating field poles. In each 
case, an induced voltage is generated.
FIGURE 11–7 Stator winding of an alternator.

Figure 11–9 shows a nonsalient rotor. This type of rotor has a smooth cylindrical surface.

reduce noise.

Power Supply for Rotor
The field windings of both salient and nonsalient rotors require DC power. Slip rings and 
brushes are used to feed the current to the windings at a potential of 100 to 250 volts DC. 
The brushes and rings are easily maintained because of the low values of field current 
encountered.
TERMINAL MARKINGS
A standard system of marking leads for field circuits has been established by ANSI (American 
National Standards Institute). The field leads for both alternators and generators are indicated 
by the markings F1 and F2. In addition, the F1 lead always connects to the positive bus of the 
DC source. (See Figures 11–5 and 11–6.)
ALTERNATOR REGULATION
Regardless of the type of generator or alternator used in a system, the terminal output voltage 
of the machine varies with any change in the load current. The impedance of the windings and 
the power factor of the load circuit both influence the regulation of an alternator. An increase in 
load current in a pure resistive load circuit causes a decrease in output voltage. A voltage drop of 
approximately 10% is common when going from a condition of no load to full load in a typical 
alternator without voltage regulation controls.
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

U N I T  1 1   T H R E E - P H A S E  A LT E R N AT O R S
For an inductive load, an increase in load current causes a greater voltage drop than is 
obtained with a pure resistive load. A load with a low value of lagging power factor produces a 
large drop in output voltage.
A capacitive load circuit produces the opposite effect. In other words, the output voltage 
rises above the no-load value with an increase in load current and is high at a low value of lead-
ing power factor.
AUTOMATIC VOLTAGE CONTROL
Unlike DC generators, alternators cannot be compounded to alter the voltage-load charac-
teristic. Moreover, output voltage variations are more likely to be severe because of changes 
in the load power factor. As a result, automatic voltage regulators generally are used with 
alternators.
Automatic voltage regulators change the alternator field current to compensate for 
any increase or decrease in the load voltage. A controller is used to increase or decrease the 
field current through the field circuit. As the AC line voltage falls, the field controller causes 
an increase in the flux and thus increases the induced voltage. An increase in the AC line 
voltage causes the controller to decrease the field current, flux, and induced voltage. Power 
companies stabilize line voltage by using a type of varying ratio transformer as a voltage 
regulator.
BRUSHLESS EXCITERS WITH SOLID-STATE VOLTAGE 
CONTROL
The permanent magnet generator (Figure 11–10) supplies high-frequency AC power input 
to the voltage regulator. Voltage and reactive current feedback information is provided to 
the regulator from potential and current transformers. Using these feedback signals and a 
reference point established by setting the voltage adjusting rheostat, the voltage regulator 
(which has a transfer switch allowing the operator to select automatic regulator control or 
manual control) provides a controlled DC output. The DC is fed to the field of the rotating 
exciter; the three-phase, high-frequency AC output is then rectified by a full-wave bridge. 
This rectified signal is applied to the main generator field. Fully rated, parallel, solid-state 
diodes with indicating fuses are provided to permit full-load generation with a diode (recti-
fier) out of service. The use of a stroboscope light permits the indicating fuses to be viewed 
during operation to determine whether a diode has failed. Figure 11–11 shows a cutaway 
view of a brushless exciter. Figure 11–12 shows the rotating components of a brushless 
excitation system.

GENERATOR TROUBLESHOOTING AND MAINTENANCE
Test the insulation resistance of the generator at initial start-up and periodically track the insu-
lation values.
Procedure for Testing Insulation Resistance
Disconnect all related components that have circuits connected to the generator windings. 
This includes the automatic voltage regulator and any diodes on the rotor. If the generator 
has resistive temperature detectors (RTDs), disconnect or isolate them from the wind-
ings circuit. Use a megohmmeter to measure the resistance of the winding insulation. The 
measurements are made from winding to winding to make sure there is no electrical path 
FIGURE 11–12 Rotating components of the 
brushless excitation system.
© Cengage Learning 2014
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

U N I T  1 1   T H R E E - P H A S E  A LT E R N AT O R S  
91
between the coil windings. Also, the measurements are taken from the coils to the grounded 
frame to check for insulation breakdown. The resistance of the insulation varies over time 
and may change if the windings are damp or in high humidity. However, the ratings should 
stay above 1.0 megohms to be considered sufficient insulation. An original reading of 100 
megohms is typical when the machine is manufactured at the factory. If the generator arrives 
and the first readings are less than 1.0 megohm, the generator may have been damaged in 
shipment or the winding may be wet.
One method to dry the winding is to run the generator unloaded and with no field excita-
tion. Run the generator for 30 minutes to allow the heat and ventilation of a running generator 
to dry the windings. Another method used to dry the windings and raise the insulation resis-
tance (IR) is to open all venting covers and blow warm air over the damp windings. Be careful 
not to overheat the windings and actually burn the insulation. Continue checking the IR value 
as the windings dry out.
A third, more risky process is to actually have the generator generate a small voltage 
and current. This should be done only if other methods are not suitable. The process is to 
short the output terminals though a shorting conductor, large enough to handle the full-load 
current output of the generator. Disconnect the normal DC excitation leads and connect a 
separate DC source of adjustable low current at 24 VDC. Start the generator and then apply 
a very small excitation current. Very carefully increase the small excitation until the current 
being monitored at the output is at approximately 80% of the full-load current rating of the 
generator. A drying out curve as in Figure 11–13 should result if you take readings of the IR 
every 30 minutes.

SUMMARY
Three-phase alternators are similar to single-phase alternators in that they can generate electri-
cal power through electromagnetic means. The rotating-field alternator is most common in 
large generating facilities. In electromechanical generation, a magnetic field is turned inside the 
housing that holds the circuit conductors. The speed of the rotating field is determined by the 
desired output frequency. The output voltage of a single generator is controlled by the strength 
of the spinning electromagnet. By adjusting the amount of DC supplied to the spinning electro-
magnetic field, the level of AC output voltage can be controlled. Some generation systems do not 
connect DC power to the rotor using brushes and slip rings; instead, they use a system called a 
brushless exciter that supplies DC to the rotor through electromagnetic induction and rectifiers.

U N I T  1 2   E N G I N E - D R I V E N  G E N E R AT I N G  S E T S
ENGINE-DRIVEN GENERATING SETS
Diesel, gasoline, or natural gas engine-driven generators are most commonly used to provide 
another source of emergency or standby power when normal utility power fails. Turbine power 
generator sets are also used in this application.
Sturdy, diesel-engine–powered generators are still popular in standby power systems. The 
use of hybrid systems using natural energy, such as the wind and the sun, is growing dramati-
cally; they are not used for emergency or standby power sources. Although diesel generators will 
not become obsolete for on-site electrical power, a need exists for alternate sources, as the role of 
the diesel-engine generator changes from a primary energy source to part of a combined source.
Most engine-driven generator sets are rated from a few hundred watts to several hundred 
kilowatts, although units rated as high as 3000 kW have been successfully applied. Multiple 
units, with some working in parallel, are becoming more commonly used to increase generating 
capacity. Controls may be manual, or automatic, depending on their application.
Transfer Switches
Elementary transfer switches are typically classified as “break before make” switching actions. 
The connection to the normal source of supply is broken before the connection is made to the 
emergency source. This action is intended to prevent the emergency generator from supply-
ing power to the normal utility source. Likewise, as the switch transfers back to normal, the 
emergency source connection is broken before the main power is reconnected. This process is 
referred to as open transition. This open transition is all right for many loads, as the automatic 
transfer takes less than 10 cycles or one-sixth of a second.
Closed transition is needed with some loads. In this situation, when the load is to be 
transferred back to the normal source, a synchronizing action must occur to synchronize the 
two sources so they can both be connected at the same time. The time that both sources are 
connected is very short, as short as 100 milliseconds, but the transfer is made without inter-
rupting the power to the load as it transfers back to normal. The first transfer, from normal to 
emergency, is still open transition.
Another variation on the transfer of load is referred to as soft loading, meaning the load is 
added gradually, or softly, to the auxiliary generator to enable loading of the on-site generation 
to meet the needs. Different modes of operation allow the soft loading to gradually transfer the 
load to the on-site generator to help reduce demand from the utility. The on-site generator can 
be used for cogeneration to sell power back to the utility and the load on the generator can be 
adjusted or programmed to stay constant to supply on-site demand as well as sell surplus power 
to the utility. This type of transfer is designed to have two sources connected simultaneously 
and the electrical load shifts from one to the other in a smooth, predictable fashion.
Depending on where the on-site generator is located, inside the building or separate from 
the building, the transfer switch can be located to efficiently serve the main normal power and 
the generator power feeds. This means that the switch may be located outdoors in an enclosure 
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

FIGURE 12–1 An automatic transfer 
switch transfers the power source for 
the electrical load from a normal supply 
to an alternate supply.
© Cengage Learning 2014
suited for the need or it may be installed indoors in a suitable enclosure. Figure 12–1 shows an 
automatic transfer switch.
An elementary diagram of a typical automatic transfer switch is shown in Figure 12–2. 
(The figure does not include engine starting and other controls.)
When the normal supply on the left side is energized, current flows from L1 through the 
time-delay coil (TD) and back to L2. After a predetermined setting of time delay in closing 
contact, the relay R coil becomes energized. Contact R then closes, and energizes the N coil. 
Power contacts N then close, supplying the load from the normal or preferred source. When the 
R coil is energized, it also opens the normally closed R contact interlock in the E coil emergency 
circuit. This safe action ensures that each power supply operates independently of the other.
When the normal power fails, all coils on the left, or normal supply side, become deener-
gized. Relay contact R drops to its normally closed position in the E (emergency coil) circuit. 
Coil E is then energized, which closes the E power contacts feeding the load from an emergency 
electrical supply.
The time delay action helps to ensure that the normal service does not supply the load 
intermittently with the emergency supply. In other words, the switch waits a preset time until 
the normal supply is firmly established, before the load is reconnected to normal.

EMERGENCY SYSTEMS
Applicable NEC and local code rules are considered when an on-site generator is selected. 
These rules differ, depending on whether the generating set is to function as a power source 
in a health care facility, such as a hospital, a standby power system, or as an emergency 
system.
On-site generator systems generally are installed wherever great numbers of people 
gather, and where artificial lighting is required, such as in hotels, theaters, sports arenas, hospi-
tals, and similar institutions. In addition to lighting, emergency systems supply loads that are 
essential to life and safety. Such installations include fire pumps, ventilation, refrigeration, and 
signaling systems. (Refer to Article 700 of the NEC.)
Optional Standby Power Generation Systems
Optional standby power generation systems include alternate power systems for applications 
such as heating, refrigeration, data processing, or communication systems where interruption 
of normal power would cause human discomfort or damage to a product in manufacturing, but 
where life and safety do not depend on the system. (Refer to Article 702 of the NEC.)
UNINTERRUPTIBLE POWER SUPPLIES
Uninterruptible power supplies (UPSs) are used as power systems to supply critical electronic 
equipment, including electronic computer and data processing equipment as described in 
Article 645—Information Technology Equipment of the NEC.

The basic system includes an electronic section that takes the normal line power and 
converts it to DC power to act as a battery charger. The system has a set of batteries that are 
constantly being charged. These batteries are also connected to an electronic inverter system to 
change the DC battery power back to normal AC power to supply an AC load. The concept is 
to constantly have battery power connected and available to provide a standard level of power 
to the electronic equipment at all times. The battery system is sized to provide the needed 
volt-amp capacity to the system for a specified period of time. The UPS system also provides 
protection from low-voltage conditions (brown-outs) and momentary surges or power deliv-
ery failures. These systems can provide continuous, filtered, and regulated power to sensitive 
equipment. The systems themselves can be sized to provide for a few computers or to supply 
hundreds of computers or other essential equipment. Figure 12–3 shows UPS control panels. 
If the UPS system is large and requires a bank of batteries to supply the uninterruptible power, 
the batteries must be installed according to NEC Article 480.

Health Care Facilities
Health care facilities are governed by several NEC rules concerning power sources, emergency 
systems, and essential electrical systems. In particular, refer to NEC Article 517. Article 700 
refers to emergency systems, and Article 701 refers to legally required standby systems.
Figure 12–4 shows a diesel-driven emergency power system consisting of four 450 kW 
electric generating sets. The system is electronically synchronized to deliver 1.8 million watts 
of emergency power for a hospital. Each unit can also be operated independently of the other 
units. Figure 12–5 shows the digital control panel for an alternator.

LEGALLY REQUIRED STANDBY SYSTEMS
NEC Article 701 states that legally required standby power systems are those systems required 
by municipal, state, federal, or other codes or a government agency having jurisdiction. In the 
event of failure of the normal power source, these systems are intended to take over automatical-
ly. Legally required standby power systems are installed to serve such loads as communication 
systems, ventilation and smoke removal systems, sewage disposal, and rescue and firefighting 
equipment, among others. These systems must be installed within the guidelines of the authority 
having jurisdiction.
COGENERATING PLANTS
Cogeneration is being used to help reduce the cost of purchasing power from a local utility. 
Many forms of cogeneration are available. Some use the concept of recovering the energy from 
some manufacturing process to drive electrical generators on site.
Some cogenerating plants are diesel-powered electric generators that are designed to 
recapture and use the waste heat from both their exhaust and cooling systems. Figure 12–6 
shows a standby generator with transfer switch and controller cabinet.
© Cengage Learning 2014
FIGURE 12–6  Diesel emergency generator for hospital system.
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

104 
U N I T  1 2   E N G I N E - D R I V E N  G E N E R AT I N G  S E T S
Although cogenerating plants are not a new concept, they are now being used to combat 
rising prices charged by public utility companies for power generation. The nation’s largest man-
ufacturers of diesel engines provide competition for the electric utility companies in the United 
States. As a result, these manufacturers have been concentrating on selling cogenerating plants.
Alongside cogeneration of electrical power is the deregulation of the electrical utility 
monopoly. This deregulation of the power generation and delivery system allows many power 
producers to supply power to the national power grid. In turn, customers can select the power 
producer that can supply the cheapest and most reliable power. In some cases, this means that 
power producers are many states distant and the local utility system provides the conductors to 
deliver the power. Many customers are installing their own on-site power generation to ensure 
that their own power supply is not interrupted because of system overloads or other problems.
Equipped with cogenerating plants, energy users may not have to rely on public utilities 
because users can make their own electricity at a lower cost and provide heating and cooling 
for their buildings.
Various technical methods have been devised for using cogenerating plants. However, 
they all capitalize on the fact that the generation of electricity wastes about twice as much 
energy in the form of heat as the amount of energy that can be generated as electricity. Steam 
heat, as a waste byproduct of manufacturing processes, is now harnessed and used to turn steam 
turbine electric alternators. This electricity, when not needed, may be sold to the public utility 
that services the plant.
As the energy-saving application of cogeneration results in greater demand for electri-
cal work, there are more jobs for electricians. It also should create a particular need for power 
generator operators with the skills to install, operate, and maintain cogenerating equipment.
SUMMARY
When the normal source of electrical power is interrupted, businesses and other organizations 
may require immediate restoration of power to continue critical operations or to sustain life 
and safe conditions. Several methods can be used to provide power, and different criteria deter-
mine which system is required or the best to use. Use the National Electrical Code to determine 
which system is appropriate to install. In addition to required systems, large power consumers 
may generate their own power on site as part of money-saving activities. This cogeneration is 
often used to reduce the amount of energy purchased from the utility or to supply the high-
energy peaks in a facility to reduce demand charges by the utility.

WHEN TO PARALLEL ALTERNATORS
Alternators are paralleled for the same reasons that make it necessary to parallel DC generators. 
Two alternators are paralleled whenever the power demand of the load circuit is greater than 
the power output of a single alternator.
When DC generators are paralleled, the output voltage and electrical polarity of the 
machines must be matched with the voltage and polarity of the line. Similar matching is 
required when alternators are paralleled. However, the matching of alternator polarity to that of 
the line presents problems not encountered when matching DC generator and line polarities. 
The output voltage of an alternator is continuously changing in both magnitude and polarity 
at a definite frequency. Thus, when two alternators are paralleled, not only must the rate of 
the rise and fall of voltage in both alternators be equal, but the rise and fall of voltage in one 
machine must be exactly in step with the rise and fall of voltage in the other machine. When two 
alternators are in step, they are said to be in synchronism. Alternators cannot be paralleled until 
their voltages, frequencies, and instantaneous polarities are nearly equal.
Figure 13–1 shows a comparison of the voltage curves of one of the phases of two three-
phase generators operating independently but at different frequencies. The voltage curves must 
be in synchronism before paralleling machines.
FIGURE 13–1 Periodic time relationship of the out-of-phase voltages of two 
 generators running at diﬀ erent frequencies.
GENERATOR #1
GENERATOR #2
© Cengage Learning 2014
The output voltage of an alternator can be controlled by varying the strength of the direct 
current in the field circuit of the alternator. A field current control can be used to vary the DC 
current. Because the frequency of an alternator varies directly with speed changes, you must be 
able to control the speed of at least one alternator in an installation containing two machines.
ACHIEVING SYNCHRONIZATION
To synchronize AC generators, several important factors must be checked:
• The phase rotation of both generator systems must be the same. Check this with lights, 
as described later, or use a phase rotation meter to determine ABC or ACB rotation.
• The AC voltages of both generators should be equal. In practice, the voltage of the 
incoming generator is usually slightly higher than that of the other operating generator.
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

U N I T  1 3   O P E R AT I O N  O F  T H R E E - P H A S E  A LT E R N AT O R S  
109
• The frequencies of the incoming generators must match when synchronized. In practice, 
the frequency of the incoming generator is 1 to 2 Hz higher than that of the on-line 
 generator. This can be observed with lights or by using a synchroscope.
The speed and output voltage of the incoming generator are slightly higher to prevent it 
from becoming a load to the system when it is connected.
Two methods of synchronization using lights are described next.
Three Dark Method
This section describes the method of synchronizing two alternators using the three dark 
method. Figure 13–2 illustrates a circuit used to parallel two three-phase alternators. Alternator 
G2 is connected to the load circuit. Alternator G1 is to be paralleled with alternator G2. Three 
lamps rated at double the output voltage to the load are connected between alternator G1 and 
the load circuit as shown. When both machines are operating, one of two effects are observed:
FIGURE 13–2 Synchronization of alternators.
G1
G2
L
L
L
SYNCHRONIZING
LAMPS
TO LOAD
CIRCUIT
© Cengage Learning 2014
• The three lamps light and go out in unison at a rate that depends on the difference in 
frequency between the two alternators.
• The three lamps light and go out at a rate that depends on the difference in frequency 
between the two machines, but not in unison. In this case, the machines are not 
 connected in the proper phase sequence and are said to be out of phase. To correct this, 
you need to interchange any two leads to alternator G1. The machines are not paral-
leled until all lamps light and go out in unison. The lamp method is shown for greater 
 simplicity of operation.
By making slight adjustments in the speed of alternator G1, the frequency of the machines 
can be equalized so that the synchronizing lamps light and go out at the lowest possible rate. 
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

110
When the three lamps are out, the instantaneous electrical polarity of the three leads from G1 
is the same as that of G2. At this instant, the voltage of G1 is equal to and in phase with that of 
G2. Now the paralleling switch can be closed so that both alternators supply power to the load. 
The two alternators are in synchronism, according to the three dark method.
The three dark method has certain disadvantages and is seldom used. A large voltage 
may be present across an incandescent lamp even though it is dark (burned out). As a result, 
it is possible to close the paralleling connection while there is still a large voltage and phase 
 difference between the machines. For small capacity machines operating at low speed, the 
phase difference may not affect the operation of the machines. However, when large capacity 
units having low armature reactance operate at high speed, a considerable amount of damage 
may result if there is a large phase difference when you attempt to parallel the units.
Two Bright, One Dark Method
Another method of synchronizing alternators is the two bright, one dark method. In this method, 
any two connections from the synchronizing lamps are crossed after the alternators are con-
nected and tested for the proper phase rotation. (The alternators are tested by the three dark 
method.) Figure 13–3(A) shows the connections for establishing the proper phase rotation by 
the three dark method. Figure 13–3(B) shows the lamp connections required to synchronize 
the alternator by the two bright, one dark method.

When the alternators are synchronized, lamps 1 and 2 are bright and lamp 3 is dark. 
Because two of the lamps are becoming brighter as one is dimming, it is easier to determine the 
moment when the paralleling switch can be closed. Furthermore, by observing the sequence 
of lamp brightness, you can tell whether the speed of the alternator being synchronized is too 
slow or too fast.

Synchroscope
A synchroscope is recommended for synchronizing two alternators because it accurately 
shows the exact instant of synchronism (Figure 13–4). The pointer rotates clockwise when 
an incoming alternator is running fast and counterclockwise when an incoming alternator 
is running slow. When the pointer is stationary, pointing upward, the alternators are syn-
chronized. Because the synchroscope is connected across one phase only, it cannot be used 
safely until the alternators have been tested and connected together for the proper phase 
rotation. Synchronizing lamps or other means must be used to determine the phase rota-
tion. In commercial applications, the alternator connections to a three-phase bus through 
a paralleling switch are permanent. This means that continuous tests for phase rotation are

Prime Movers
In industrial applications, alternators are driven by various types of prime movers such as steam 
turbines, water turbines, and internal combustion engines. For applications on ships, alterna-
tors often are driven by DC motors. Regardless of how alternators are driven, speed variation 
is a factor in paralleling the machines. Thus, the electrician should have a basic knowledge of 
speed governors and other speed-regulating devices. This text, however, does not detail the 
operation of these mechanical devices.
PARALLELING ALTERNATORS
Because apprentices are likely to be required to parallel alternators driven by DC motors 
sometime in their instruction, the following steps outline the procedure for paralleling these 
machines. Figure 13–5 illustrates a typical circuit for paralleling two three-phase alternators.
Procedure
 
1. Set the field rheostat (R2) of alternator G2 to the maximum resistance position.
 
2. Knowing the number of field poles in alternator G2, determine the speed required 
to generate the desired frequency.
 
3. Energize the prime mover to bring alternator G2 up to the required speed.
 
4. Set Switch S3 to read the AC voltage across one phase of G2. Adjust field rheostat 
R2 until the output voltage is equal to the rated voltage of the load circuit.
 
5. Close the load switch and switch S4 to feed the load circuit. Readjust the speed 
of the prime mover to maintain the predetermined speed required for the desired 
frequency.
 
6. Readjust R2 to obtain the rated AC voltage of the load circuit.
 
7. Energize the prime mover to drive the second alternator, G1. Adjust the speed of 
the alternator to the approximate value required to match the frequencies of the 
alternators.
 
8. Set switch S3 to measure the AC voltage across one phase of G1. Adjust field 
 rheostat R1 until the AC voltage is equal at either position of switch S3. The 
 voltage output of both alternators is now equal.

9. (Phase Rotation.) With paralleling switch S2 open, close switch S1. The three sets 
of lamps across the terminals of the open switch respond in one of two ways:
 
 
a. The three lamps brighten and then dim in unison.
 
 
b.  Two lamps brighten in unison as the remaining lamp dims. Then the two bright 
lamps dim as the dark lamp brightens.
 
10. If the lamps respond as in 9(a), the alternators are connected for the proper 
phase rotation. The operator then may proceed to step 12 in synchronizing the 
alternators.
 
11. If the lamps respond as in 9(b), the alternators are not in the proper phase 
 rotation. To correct the condition, interchange any two alternator leads at the 
 terminals of switch S2. All three lamps should dim together and brighten together. 
No attempt to parallel the alternators should be made until the lamps respond in 
this manner.
 
12. The three lamp sets flicker (dim and brighten) at a rate equal to the frequency 
 difference between the two alternators. Adjust the speed control of prime mover 
Ml to make the lamps flicker at the lowest possible rate.
 
13. Interchange two lamp set leads (not alternator leads) at the terminals of switch 
S2 so that the alternators can be synchronized using the two bright, one dark 
method.
 
14. Again adjust the field rheostat of alternator G1 until both alternators have the same 
output voltage as measured at either position of the voltmeter switch S3.
 
15. With one hand on switch S2, watch the lamps. Close the switch at the exact instant 
that two lamps are at their brightest and the other lamp is out. This operation 
shunts out the synchronizing lamps and parallels the alternators.
 
16. Ammeters I1 and I2 indicate the amount of load current carried by each alternator. 
If the load circuit has a unity power factor, then the sum of the ammeter readings 
should equal the reading of the ammeter in the load circuit.
 
17. Note that a change in the field excitation of either alternator does not apprecia-
bly change the amount of current supplied to the system. Such a change in field 
excitation does, however, affect the power factor of the specific alternator. The 
field rheostat of each machine should be adjusted to the highest power factor as 
indicated by the lowest value of current from the individual machine. Increasing 
or decreasing the mechanical power to either alternator increases or decreases 
the load current of that machine. As a result, the division of the load between the 
alternators can be changed by slight changes in the alternator speed.

Speed versus Load Characteristics
Two alternators operating in parallel must have the same frequency and the same terminal 
 voltage. In addition, the prime movers of the parallel alternators must have similar drooping 
speed load characteristics. For steam-, diesel-, water-, or gas-driven prime movers, the speed 
load characteristic depends on adjustments of a mechanical speed control governor. These 
adjustments determine the division of load for two alternators operating in parallel. For this 
reason, the kilowatt load delivered by two alternators in parallel cannot be divided in any 
desired proportion by varying the DC field excitation of either machine.
Two alternators properly connected in parallel operate in stable equilibrium. If one alter-
nator attempts to pull out of synchronism, a current is created that circulates between both 
alternators. This current increases the speed of the lagging machine and retards the leading 
machine, thus preventing the machines from pulling out of synchronism.
REVERSE POWER
If, for any reason, one machine is allowed to slow to a point where the other machine is taking 
all the electrical load, the zero load generator then goes to a negative value, or “reverse power.” 
This generator has now become a motor. This situation is of particular concern where the 
machine’s protective scheme has not been designed to operate properly in the motoring situ-
ation. In such conditions, reverse-current relays are usually employed to trip the generator on 
detection of reverse power flow.
Results of Motorization of a Generator
If a generator loses prime mover power, it acts as a motor with a DC field on the rotor. The 
DC field causes the rotor to try and follow the AC field in the same direction as before. If 
the mechanical drag on the rotor is heavy, it will fall behind and “slip poles,” inducing a large 
voltage into the rotor; this can cause insulation breakdown of the windings, flashover at the 
brushes, and violent shaking of the generator mountings.
If a generator loses DC excitation to the rotor it does not generate, but the prime mover 
power still turns the rotor. Now the generator acts as a motor running at no load.
SUMMARY
In many cases, the parallel operation of alternators is essential to provide needed power and 
to maintain electrical power during peak loads or when removing an alternator from service 
for maintenance. The requirements for paralleling are (1) the phase rotation of the generated 
voltage must be the same, (2) the voltage at the paralleling point must be the same, and (3) 
the frequencies of the generators must match. Lamps and synchroscopes are often used to aid 
in the paralleling procedure. If a generator loses output power, it must be removed from the 
electrical power system, or serious consequences could result.

U N I T  1 4   W I R I N G  F O R  A LT E R N AT O R S
This unit presents the control panel and equipment for a three-phase, 2400-volt alterna-
tor. The circuits and connections covered in detail are the direct-current field circuit and all 
control equipment; the alternating-current, three-phase output circuit with associated switch-
gear; and the connections for the instruments and instrument transformers used in a common 
installation.
DIRECT-CURRENT CIRCUIT FOR FIELD EXCITATION
When alternators are connected so that they are separately excited, a DC bus must be used to 
deliver the DC power for field excitation to multiple generators. This system is used so that if 
one alternator fails, other alternators may still use the DC bus for excitation. In this case, the DC 
bus is connected to each of the alternators supplying power to the system. Each generator con-
trol panel has individual controls to monitor and adjust the DC power taken from the bus and 
delivered to the alternator’s field. In this way, each alternator’s output voltage can be controlled 
and the power factor of the output power can be adjusted by the field control. In some genera-
tion systems, switches open and close the DC power to the field. If there are manual switches, 
the switch must contain some method of discharging the DC field as it is opened. If there is no 
system for discharging the field, the DC field that collapses in the field coil generates very high 
voltages in the field coil (inductive kick concept) and the field windings may be destroyed. In 
some generator systems, a large diode system is installed to prevent field discharge damage. 
Under a normal connection, the diode is reverse biased and does not conduct. In the event of 
the magnetic field collapse, the diode becomes forward biased and conducts current back to the 
winding, reduces the inductive kick effect, and prevents field breakdown.
Figure 14–1 illustrates the connections required for the separately excited field circuit of 
an alternator. Note that when the field discharge switch is open, the auxiliary blade closes to 
complete a path through the field discharge resistor. Thus, any inductive voltage in the alterna-
tor field is discharged through the field discharge resistor to prevent damage. The field rheostat 
is connected so that it is not in the discharge circuit.
ALTERNATOR OUTPUT CIRCUIT
The alternator in the installation described in this unit is rated at 2400 volts, three phase. The 
three-phase, 2400-volt output of the alternator is fed to the switchboard through a three-wire, 
high-voltage lead cable in galvanized rigid conduit. The three conductors are fed through an oil-
type circuit breaker, current transformers, and disconnect switches to the three-phase bus bars. 
An oil-type circuit breaker (switch) is used because of the relatively high voltage of the alterna-
tor. As the contacts of this switch open, any arc is immediately quenched in insulating oil.
Figure 14–2 illustrates an electrically operated oil switch (circuit breaker). Note that each 
of the three sets of contactors is mounted in a separate cell or tank that is filled with an insulat-
ing oil. The three sets of contactors thus open and close in oil. The figure also shows a contactor

assembly for one pole of a three-pole oil switch. Note the closing coil and the trip coils. The 
closing coil is relatively large and has a very fast positive action; the trip coil is smaller in size. 
The trip coil actuates a trip latch that causes the oil switch contactors to open.
The control circuit for the oil switch in most alternator installations is connected to a DC 
source such as a bank of batteries. If there is a complete failure of the AC power, the oil switch 
can still be operated from the DC source, as is true of other emergency circuits.
A small switch handle located on the switchboard is used to actuate the control circuit. 
Two indicating lamps also are mounted on the switchboard. One of the indicating lamps is 
green and is on when the oil switch is open. The second indicating lamp is red and is on when 
the oil switch is closed. The red lamp normally is located directly above the control switch 
handle, and the green lamp is located below the switch handle.

and closes its contacts to establish a path through the DC closing coil. The three main sets of oil 
switch contacts also change at this time. When the main closing relay is energized, the normally 
closed M contacts open. In addition, the green pilot lamp circuit opens, and the two normally 
open M contacts close. The red indicating lamp is now on. When the on button is released, the 
oil switch remains in the on position because it is secured by a mechanical latch mechanism.
When the off button is pressed, the DC trip coil is energized to trip the latch mechanism. 
The oil switch contacts thus open to the off position. As a result, the red indicating lamp goes 
out, and the green indicating lamp lights.
The control handle and indicating lamps for an oil switch generally are mounted on the 
switchboard. The oil switch itself, however, is usually located separately from the switchboard 
room.
Current transformers are used to step down the current in the output leads of the alter-
nator to a value that can be used in instrument circuits. Step-down current transformers also 
insulate the low-voltage instrument circuit from the high-current primary circuit. The second-
ary current rating of a current transformer is 5 amperes (see Unit 23). The current rating of 
the primary winding of the transformer must be high enough to handle the maximum current 
delivered by the alternator.
The alternator output leads feed from the current transformers to disconnect switches 
and then to the three-phase bus bars. A disconnect switch is a form of knife switch that is 
opened with a switch stick while exposed to air. The disconnect switches are operated only 
after the alternator oil switch is opened. The operator must wear rubber gloves when using an 
approved switch stick to open the disconnect switches. Never open disconnect switches under 
load—this is the purpose of the oil switch. It is designed to interrupt the arc without damage 
to the switch blade.
In most alternator installations, the three-phase bus bars are energized constantly. 
Because the disconnect switches disconnect the oil switch and the alternator from the bus bars, 
the alternator can be shut down and the disconnect switches opened to permit maintenance 
work on the oil switch under safe conditions. When the alternator requires maintenance or 
repair work, the disconnect switches are pulled to the off position even though the oil switch 
is open. The reason for this precaution is that the insulating oil in the oil switch may have 
become carbonized. The carbonized oil can act as a partial conductor, resulting in a feedback 
from the live 2400-volt bus bars through the oil switch and carbonized oil to the alternator 
terminals. Remember that the disconnect switches and the oil switch must be open when any 
maintenance or repair work is to be done on AC generators. The generators should also be 
shut down.
Figure 14–4 is a wiring diagram of typical alternator connections to the three-phase 
bus bars.
The three bus bars for the AC output of the alternator are mounted on insulators 
because the bus bars have a potential difference of 2400 volts between them. It is important

that the proper air gap be maintained between the three bus bars and that adequate clearance 
be  provided between the bus bars and the ceiling and side walls of the room. Barriers shall be 
placed in all service switchboards to isolate the service bus bars and terminals from the remain-
der of the switchboard.
Large generators are constructed in two styles. One style uses a separate exciter DC 
generator and feeds the DC excitation field into the alternator rotor through brushes and 
slip rings. Because the field current and voltage are relatively low compared to the output

of the alternator, brushes and slip rings work well. The other style of large generation 
equipment uses a brushless exciter style to supply DC to the rotor. Either method is effec-
tive and accomplishes the same task—to provide a DC field to the rotating field of the AC 
generator.
To adjust the field and provide the desired output voltage, the output voltage levels must 
be monitored. In the brush-type rotor connection, the AC is monitored at the output, and a DC 
field of a small DC separately excited generator is controlled. As the output voltage drops, the 
DC field is increased. This small DC generator, called an amplydine, supplies the DC field to a 
larger DC exciter generator. The second generator then supplies DC to the alternator’s field. 
This process allows for stages of amplification of the DC field. A small change in output AC 
affects the DC field to the amplydine that feeds the second stage of amplification for the DC to 
the alternator field. A small control voltage at the amplydine level is used to control the large 
DC to the rotor of the generator.
Brushless exciters are discussed in Unit 11. The concept is to use a small amount of con-
trollable DC, and then amplify it and feed it to the alternator field. This process uses semicon-
ductors to change induced AC into DC on the rotor. Figure 14–5 shows a block diagram of the 
two styles of field control.
ALTERNATOR COOLING
The electrical current flowing in the alternators through conductors produces heat in the 
coils because of I2 R losses. This heat must be removed from the alternator to maintain 
operations. For most small alternators, the windings are air cooled by a fan on the rotor that 
pushes air through slots in the armature winding core. If the alternators are indoors, means 
must be provided to vent the heat as the generators run. In large utility generators, air cool-
ing is not enough. In these circumstances, hydrogen is used for cooling the alternator core. 
Using hydrogen for cooling has the advantage of being less dense than air so that it reduces 
the windage losses in the alternator cooling process. Hydrogen also absorbs and removes 
more heat than air, which provides more efficient cooling. This hydrogen system is normally 
passed around the output voltage bus as well, to provide cooling for the output conductors 
of the alternator.
INSTRUMENT CIRCUITS
The voltage to the potential coils of instruments mounted on the switchboard should not 
exceed 120 to 125 volts. The voltage coils of wattmeters, watt-hour meters, and voltmeters 
usually are designed for a maximum voltage of 150 volts. Because the three-phase output of 
the alternator is 2400 volts, two instrument potential transformers connected in open delta 
are required to step down the voltage to 120 volts, three phase (see Unit 23). The potential 
transformers are small in size because the load on the low-voltage secondary is very small. Each 
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

potential transformer is rated at 100 to 200 volt-amperes (VA). For the installation shown in 
Figure 14–6, the load on the secondary of the transformer consists of the potential coils of the 
kilowatt meter and the voltmeter. The instrument potential transformers are rated at 2400 
volts on the high-voltage side and 120 volts on the low-voltage side. The low voltage at the

Figure 14–6 shows a wiring diagram for most of the instruments and instrument trans-
formers described. The current in the secondary of current transformer circuits is never in 
excess of 5 amperes. Therefore, either 14 or 12 AWG wire is used on the rear of the switchboard.
For most permanent switchboard installations, the scale readings on the instruments 
are graduated to include the voltage and current transformer multipliers. This means that any 
error made by the switchboard operator in applying instrument multipliers is automatically 
e liminated.
Two instruments not shown in the wiring diagram in Figure 14–6 are the voltmeter and the 
synchroscope. In typical installations, several alternators may be operating in parallel. Each alterna-
tor has a separate panel, and these panels are mounted next to one another to make up a complete 
switchboard. One voltmeter and one synchroscope are then mounted on a movable panel located 
at the end of the switchboard. The position of this panel can be adjusted so that the voltmeter and 
synchroscope are visible from any one of the generator control panels. A voltmeter switch located 
on each generator panel gives the operator a means of connecting the voltmeter to measure the 
voltage output of any alternator. In addition, special synchronizing switches permit the use of one 
synchroscope to synchronize any one of several alternators to the three-phase system.
Figure 14–7 shows the circuit connections for the voltmeter and synchroscope. Figure 14-6 
indicates that the voltmeter switch has three positions. The voltmeter can be connected across 
any one of the three voltages of an alternator. If the voltage of a second alternator must be 
measured, the voltmeter switch is turned to the off position. The switch handle or key is then 
removed and inserted in the voltmeter switch of the second AC generator. Again, the switch 
may be turned to any one of the three voltage positions. Thus, one voltmeter can be used to 
measure the three voltages of each of several AC generators controlled through the switchboard.
A synchroscope switch is mounted on each alternator panel. When the switch handle is 
turned to the incoming position, the synchroscope is connected to the secondary voltage of 
one phase of an alternator being synchronized with the AC system. The synchroscope switch 
of a second alternator, which is already paralleled with the three-phase system, is connected to 
the run position. Thus, one coil winding of the synchroscope is energized from the running bus 
bars. The other winding of the synchroscope is energized from the incoming bus bars. With 
these connections, the synchroscope indicates the extent that the incoming machine is out of 
phase. When the incoming alternator is in phase with the three-phase system and the alternator 
voltage is equal to that of the bus bars, the control switch can be turned to the on position. As a 
result, the oil switch contactors close, and the alternator is paralleled with the bus bars. The oil 
circuit breaker is used to connect and disconnect the alternator when it is running under load. 
This ensures safe operation and prolongs switch contact life.
Many of the meters on generators are now in digital format. This allows the user to more 
precisely determine the exact currents and voltages being produced. The use of these meters 
is still supplemented by the synchroscope to verify the exact point of synchronism if manual 
paralleling of the alternators is needed.

POWER SOURCES
Wind, fuel cells, microturbine, solar photovoltaic power, and flywheel reserve power are all 
alternate sources of electric power. These systems are relatively small sources compared 
to coal or nuclear plants. They produce much less power than a hydroelectric generating 
station and often less than oil-fired steam generation or diesel peaking plants. These small 
generation methods are often used to reduce the demand on the power grid that is normally 
supplied from utility power stations. Sometimes these sources are used in remote locations 
where the power grid is weak or nonexistent. Very often these sources are used to power 
 specific applications, reduce the power needs from the utility, or supplement the utility 
 supply capacity.
Wind Power
As more energy sources are sought, the trend is to convert other sources of energy (such as 
wind) to electric power and then distribute the electricity to where it is needed.
Wind-powered generators are becoming increasing popular for sources of power with 
no direct charge for the source of energy. Unlike the workhorses of commercial generation, 
such as coal and nuclear power, where the procurement, transportation, storage, and waste 
are problems that must be dealt with, wind is free of these drawbacks. The third major 
source of power (after coal and nuclear power) is water or hydroelectric generation, in 
which the resource is free and available as long as it rains. Hydroelectric power generation 
requires a source of water supply, a topography with a large vertical drop for falling water, 
and storage of the potential energy behind hydroelectric dams, which creates its own sets 
of problems.
Generating Power from Wind
The commercial generation of power derived from the wind is a simple concept, but the tech-
nology has improved greatly in the recent past, making it more feasible for economical produc-
tion. Wind-generation “farms” are built after careful studies indicate that enough consistent 
wind is available to make an investment in the towers, turbines, generators, and transformers 
needed to provide power for the power grid. These “farms” have many towers lined up like 
“crops” to harvest the wind.
Wind power has the benefits of cheap, renewable energy, and no waste or storage 
problems. The drawback is, of course, that the wind must blow to produce electricity. In 
many parts of the world and in the United States, some regions have significant wind power 
available on a consistent basis, which makes them good candidates for wind-powered gen-
eration. For the most part, these systems supplement the base load generators providing 
energy locally or to the power grid as “extra” electric power, so the base load can reduce its 
power output.

U N I T  1 5   A LT E R N AT I V E  P O W E R  S O U R C E S

Wind Technology
Because wind-powered generators come in many different sizes and therefore capacities, the 
 production and uses of the power vary. Large-scale non–wind-generating stations, such as utility 
producers, typically use synchronous generators where the generator speed can be easily con-
trolled. The electrical wind-generation industry cannot count on consistent wind speed to turn 
the generators at precise speeds. (See Figure 15–1 for large wind generation.) Most wind genera-
tors have minimum and maximum operating speeds and therefore maximum and minimum wind 
speed curves for operation. Below these speeds, the generator does not produce enough useful 
power and is taken off line. As seen in the figure, there is a maximum wind speed that produces use-
ful power. As the wind speed exceeds the upper level, the generator wind turbine may be damaged, 
and the turbine is shut down and the blades locked to prevent damage to the blades or the rotor.
FIGURE 15–1 Wind-generation farms converting wind energy to electrical energy.
© Cengage Learning 2014
Different generation schemes are used in the wind electric generation industry. If the gen-
erators are synchronous generators used by the wind industry, then the mode of operation and

but this is a challenging problem.

to a rectifier assembly and then to an inverter to produce the desired voltage and frequency to 
the output grid. The conversion process can be expensive for large machines. See Figure 15–4.

Currently, a large portion of large wind generation is provided by asynchronous or 
induction generators. An induction generator works by the common principle of spinning 
a magnetic field within a stationary stator. If the stator is connected to an AC source of the 
grid, the stator magnetic field spins at a synchronous speed dictated by the stator frequency. 
If the rotor has a mechanical input and spins at the same speed as the stator field, no volt-
age is induced into the rotor. If the rotor spins inside the stator at greater than synchronous 
speed, then the rotor works as a rotating field generator and has induced voltage into the 
stator. If the rotor spins slower than synchronous speed, the machine acts like a motor 
and current is inducted through induction into the rotor and consumes power from the 
grid. At this point, the wind generator must be disconnected to avoid being a load to the

Doubly Fed Induction Generator (DFIG)
A wound-rotor motor–type of machine can be used a generator. (See information on wound-
rotor motors in Electricity 4.) In wound-rotor motors there are connections to the stator (per-
manent connections), and there are connections to the windings of the rotor through slip rings 
(rotating connections). Compared to a synchronous generator, the DFIG can have a wider 
range of rotor speeds—up to 10% over synchronous speed. At the lower speed range, the DFIG 
can still generate power where a synchronous generator cannot. This extended speed range is 
accomplished by more control on the DFIG and is at a higher cost, but is economical because 
of the overall power production range. By making connections to the rotor, the rotor current 
and induced voltage occurs when the rotor spins faster than the synchronous speed of the sta-
tor, which is connected to a 60 Hz grid system. For instance, if the stator frequency is 60 Hz 
and it is a four-pole, three-phase machine, the synchronous speed of the rotating field is 1800 
rpm. If the rotor spins at the same speed, no voltage is induced. If the rotor spins faster, then 
magnetic induction takes place and a voltage is induced into the rotor windings as if it were a

rotating armature machine. Connect the rotor windings to a load, and the output of the rotor 
can be used. Typically the rotor output voltage is rectified and then inverted to the desired load 
frequency and voltage.
If the rotor is connected to an AC supply, the output of the rotor may be negated or 
canceled by a counter-electromotive force CEMF (opposite voltage) that, in effect, opposes 
the rotor output and creates a zero current output total. At this point, no voltage is produced 
or induced into the stator. By increasing the rotor CEMF, the spinning rotor induces back into 
the stator of the wound-rotor motor. Because the rotor current produces a field that is inducing 
into the stator, the synchronous frequency of the stator is slightly reduced. However, the stator 
is still connected to a power grid and still produces power at the new lower frequency. This pro-
cess of feeding both the stator and the rotor of the wound-rotor–type machine creates a doubly 
fed induction generator. At rotor speeds below sync speed, the rotor takes some power from the 
grid and uses it as excitation, as is seen in a rotating armature–type generator. The rotor torque 
produced by the wind power continues to generate electricity.
If the generators are asynchronous, then the control mode may be squirrel-cage mode, 
variable slip mode, or variable slip with DFIG mode.
The variable slip with the DFIG needs more complex control. This type of generation 
produces a majority of the output power (about two-thirds) from the stator and a smaller 
amount (one-third) from the rotor. The stator can be connected to the grid through transform-
ers to match the voltage, but the rotor voltage and frequency must be rectified and inverted to 
match the grid. See Figure 15–6.

Small Wind Systems
For small wind turbine systems—generating up to and including 100 kW—the systems are less 
complicated and are covered in NEC Article 694. The majority of these small systems are direct-
drive, permanent magnet induction generators. These generators generally have a maximum 
voltage of 600 volts when connected to one- or two-family dwellings. They may have higher 
output if they comply with Part IX of Article 694. Any live parts over 150 volts to ground must 
only be accessible to qualified persons when the system is energized. The nacelle is the enclo-
sure for the turbine and alternator. Where installing the tower and support structure, follow the 
requirements in Article 690.4.
Article 694.12 determines circuit sizes based on current. The turbine output circuit cur-
rents are based on the maximum power output the generator is designed to produce. As most 
of these generators produce a varying output voltage and frequency, the generator output is 
fed to a rectifier system that converts the AC to a DC supply. The output of the rectifier is 
then fed to an inverter (much like the process for VFDs), and the inverter output is tied to 
an output system. The circuit ampacity of the output of the inverter shall be based on the 
continuous output current rating. If the system is a stand-alone (not connected to a grid), 
the input current to the inverter shall be that current that the inverter can use to supply an 
output at the lowest input voltage. The current values obtained shall be considered continu-
ous currents. The conductors for the system shall be sized to carry 125% of the currents, as 
calculated above.
Article 694.18 refers to stand-alone systems. The premises wiring system designed to 
supply a premises shall have the same requirement as if it were supplied by a typical service. 
The supply side of the stand-alone service can be modified by NEC 694.18(A–D). The inverter 
output is allowed to supply less than the full calculated load on a disconnect. The inverter sup-
ply must not be less than the largest single utilization load on the system. The size of the circuit 
conductors between the inverter and the structure disconnect shall be based on the rating of 
the inverter output. The output can feed just 120 volts to a structure if following the guideline 
of Article 394.18C.
Wind-generation systems often have storage systems, meaning batteries. If the differ-
ent power systems are interconnected—having a utility feed and power from the wind—
then, according to NEC Article 694.50, all points of interconnection must be marked at an 
accessible location at the disconnecting means with voltage and current ratings. Plaques 
must also indicate the location of both energy system disconnects. If the wind generation 
is interactive, then the inverter is tied to the utility grid. These inverters must be listed 
as identified to be interactive, which means they will automatically disconnect the wind 
 system from the utility line if the utility has no power. As required, the frequency and 
 voltage levels must be the same as the grid, and the power must be synchronized as the 
power is reconnected.
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

U N I T  1 5   A LT E R N AT I V E  P O W E R  S O U R C E S 
Battery storage is used to supply power to the premises when no wind is blowing. 
These batteries typically require charging controllers to help regulate battery charging. See 
NEC Article 694.75 for alternatives. A diversion charge controller is used to either charge the 
 batteries or supply the load. It is required to protect the battery system from overcharging. The 
charging power can be sent to the batteries to bring them to full charge, and then the energy 
above that which is needed is “dumped” or diverted. These controllers were sometimes referred 
to as dump controllers now called diversion controllers. The excess energy was either used to 
heat water, run lights, or for some other nonessential function. Now we say that we divert the 
energy to another function. According to the Code, you need two reliable loads to divert to, but 
these loads cannot be the utility connection. These alternative energy systems are continually 
 changing and are going through technical and regulatory revisions.
Fuel Cells
Fuel cells generate electrical power through a chemical reaction. Fuel cells are becoming 
more abundant and more economical as an alternative energy solution. Recently, more heav-
ily populated states in the Northeast and the Western United States have been using fuel cell 
power production to provide temporary standby power. In the event of power grid failure by 
storm or accident, the power is restored to cell towers and essential communication points 
by using local fuel cell power production. Fuel cells have been used for years for small-scale 
electrical power needs and have been used in vehicles ranging from the space shuttle to city 
buses, trucks, and passenger cars. Different types of fuel cells exist that produce DC electric 
power by using different types of electrolytes and different compositions of cathode and 
anode.
The basic concept of all fuel cells is the same in that they use hydrogen as a fuel. 
Hydrogen is plentiful but needs to be processed for fuel cell use. As hydrogen enters the fuel 
cell at the anode, a chemical reaction occurs that pulls the electron away from the hydrogen 
atom. These stripped electrons are the electron current source for the external electrical 
circuit. The positive hydrogen ions are now in the electrolyte (see Figure 15–7). Another 
resource that is needed to complete the chemical reaction is oxygen, which enters the cathode. 
The oxygen is taken from the surrounding air. As the electrons return from the electric circuit, 
they recombine with the hydrogen ions and the oxygen atoms to create H2O, or water. The 
waste from this reaction is water, which is sent down a drain. The other byproduct of the reac-
tion is heat. This heat is often captured and used for other needs such as water heating or space 
heating, or even cooling using absorption-type chillers, making the fuel cell very efficient. The 
process of using the electrical DC output for customer use usually requires that the power be 
inverted through an electronic system much like the frequency-producing components of a 
variable frequency drive. See Figure 15–8 for an operating fuel cell producing 5 kW of power 
for a customer site.

Types of Fuel Cells
Different types of fuel cells have specific advantages and disadvantages. Alkaline fuel cells 
use potassium hydroxide in water as the electrolyte. They are about 70% efficient and oper-
ate at about 300–400° Fahrenheit (149–204°C). These cells have small electrical outputs, 
from 300 W to 1000 kW; however, they need pure hydrogen fuel and pure oxygen, and 
have expensive platinum electrodes. Molten carbonate fuel cells use high-temperature salts 
for an electrolyte and can leak. These fuel cells are 60–80% efficient and can produce up to 
2 mW. They use an inexpensive nickel electrode. The major drawback is the high heat of 
approximately 1200°F (649°C). Because it takes time to reach operating temperature, the 
cell is best used for continuous load under supervision because of the high heat. Phosphoric 
acid cells have a lower operating temperature—300–400°F (149–204°C)—and have an 
efficiency of about 60%. The electrolyte is phosphoric acid, which allows the cell to use less 
concentrated fuels and comes in sizes from 200 kW to 11 mW. Proton exchange membrane 
(PEM) fuel cells are about 45% efficient and operate at about 174°F (79°C). The electro-
lyte is a solid membrane that does not leak, which makes PEMs suitable for mobile units 
between 50 W and 200 kW. The hydrogen fuel must be purified and the electrodes are 
expensive platinum.
NEC Article 692 Fuel Cell Systems
Article 692.2 provides the definitions needed to determine the NEC requirements for fuel cell 
installations. Because fuel cells are often supplementary power sources, the Code requires that 
a permanent plaque or directory that lists all the power sources be established at each ser-
vice location. Article 692.4(C) states that fuel cell systems shall only be installed by qualified 
 persons. The fuel cell must be evaluated and listed for the intended purpose, and the listed cur-
rent on the nameplate shall be the circuit current rating. The feeder conductors that connect 
the fuel cell to the premises wiring must be sized on the nameplate or the rating of the integral 
overcurrent protection on the fuel cell.
Article 692.10(C) states that a fuel cell that is a stand-alone, meaning that the system pro-
vides power independently of an electrical distribution network, and that only supplies a single-
phase, 120/240-volt panel board with 120 volts, where there are no 240-volt loads, must be 
 posted with a sign that warns that no multiwire branch circuits can be attached. NEC Chapter 3—
Wiring Methods are permitted to be used with the fuel cell installations as well as any specific 
equipment listed for fuel cells. Article 692.41(B) dictates how to ground systems that are not 
“stand-alone” systems.
When the fuel cell is used to feed power back to a supply grid, the fuel cell must be able to 
detect when the utility power is lost. If the supply is lost, the fuel cell must not feed power back 
to the supply grid. This is a safety feature for utility line workers who would assume the power 
is off and do not want power to backfeed on to their lines.

Microturbine
Microturbines are another source of local power that can be used for stand-alone power at a site 
or as cogeneration that is supplied back to a power grid. As the name implies, microturbines are 
generators driven by mechanical turbines that are small scale compared to the large commercial 
generators. The microturbine consists of a system with a compressor, a combustor, a turbine, 
and a permanent magnet generator. The turbine is designed to spin at a very high speed: 
96,000 RPM. This turns the rotor with a mounted two-pole permanent magnet inside the fixed 
armature. The generator actually produces a variable frequency, up to 1600 Hz AC output volt-
age, which is rectified and fed to a DC bus section of the output. The power is then inverted 
through an IGBET inverter to produce a typical output of three-phase, 480-volt, 60 Hz output. 
Microturbines are typically 30–60 kW each, so larger loads would need two or more systems 
connected via paralleling gear.
Microturbines are meant to run continuously for up to a year, then are removed from 
service for minor maintenance, and then restarted. These systems are designed to run on “air 
bearings,” so there is very little friction and no need for bearing replacement or lubrication, or 
coolants. They can be fueled by natural gas, propane, diesel, or kerosene. They can even use 
“waste gas” as a fuel. Waste gas may be methane from sewage treatment, landfill venting, agri-
cultural digesters, and so forth.
Solar Photovoltaic
Solar photovoltaic systems have become more popular as photovoltaic (PV) cells have 
become more efficient and costs have been reduced. Many individual cells are connected 
together to make up PV modules or panels. As the panels are assembled, the individual cells 
may be connected in series, which raises the voltage of the panel (series circuit rules). The 
individual cells may also be connected in parallel, which raises the total current of the panel, 
but not the voltage (parallel circuit rules). The panels are typically rated by output watts 
and the output voltage and current at nominal values. (See Figure 15–9 for an example.) A 
system of panels assembled into systems to collect sunlight and create electricity is called 
an array.
