U N I T  1 5   A LT E R N AT I V E  P O W E R  S O U R C E S 
The actual photovoltaic cells are of three main types. The first type, consisting of single 
crystalline, multicrystalline, or amorphous-type silicon cells, is about 15% efficient. The second 
type of cell is produced as a polycrystalline thin film, which is about 10% efficient, with copper 
indium gallium di-selenide (CIGS) or cadmium telluride (CdTe). A third type is thin film sili-
con with gallium arsenide. Each type of cell has its own advantages and disadvantages. For all 
photocells, the efficiency of conversion falls as the temperature rises. This has been improved 
by the development of newer cells, such as heterojunction intrinsic thin film (HIT) by Sanyo, 
which have high efficiency at higher temperatures. Cells typically produce low voltage—about 
0.5 volt and between 1 and 6 amps.
As panels are assembled, the number and type of cells determine the output of the panels. 
The typical output voltages are between 12 and 60 volts. The output currents vary according to 
the number of panels connected. NEC Article 690.7 refers to the maximum photovoltaic system 
voltage for open circuit voltage, which is highest at no current draw. In reference to the system 
temperature, the lowest expected ambient temperature produces the highest output voltage. 
If the type of cell is crystalline or multicrystalline, then a correction factor must be applied to 
the open circuit voltage if the ambient temperatures are expected to be below 77°F (25°C). As 
noted in the NEC article, if the ambient temperature is below –40°F (–40°C), or if other cell 
materials are used, then the manufacturer’s instructions should be followed. The voltages thus 
obtained should be used for the voltage ratings of PV connecting cables. The current ratings 
of these same cables is determined by the sum of the individual panel’s short circuit (0 volts) 
output current that is connected in parallel.
The PV system includes the power controllers, or inverters, with arrays or subarrays. An 
array is an assembly of mounted panels with a support structure that may be either permanent 
or a moveable system that tracks the sunlight for optimum amount of direct sunlight during the 
day. These arrays may be monopole, meaning they have one set of power leads, one positive 
and one negative. They may also be bipolar arrays, which have two output leads. One lead is 
positive to a common point, or ground reference, and the other lead is negative with respect 
to the common lead. These arrays or subarrays have to be connected in a component called a 
combiner box in order to provide the desired power for the inverters or other system compo-
nents. As the name implies, the arrays or panels are combined to create a single supply of power. 
NEC Article 690.4 provides rules for installation of the PV systems, which state that PV conduc-
tors and non-PV system conductors must not be contained in the same raceway, or they must 
be separated by a partition in a box, cable tray, or gutter. Photovoltaic system conductors must 
be grouped and labeled as per Article 690.4 B(1–4). If the output voltage of monopole subar-
rays exceeds the voltage as part of a bipolar system, then the leads from the monopole arrays 
must be kept separated until they are connected to the inverter.
Another component of the PV systems is the inverter, which is used to bring the DC 
voltage produced by the PV array to a useable AC voltage with the desired frequency by using 
electronics, much like a variable frequency drive (VFD). The term string inverter refers only to

the method of connecting the panels in strings or series, to increase the voltage of the system 
without increasing the current. The voltage may be up to 800 VDC. A string of arrays can be 
connected in series to a combiner box and to an inverter (string inverter), using a smaller wire 
size and keeping the voltage high to deliver large amounts of power. Another type of inverter is 
a central inverter. In this case, a single inverter, rather than several smaller inverters, is used to 
create AC voltage. These may be used on larger power systems or may be a more economical 
method for a particular system design. (See Figures 15–10[A] and 10[B]). Inverters are often 
used as utility interactive devices, meaning that the inverter connects the local PV generation 
system to the utility in order to sell power to the serving utility. The name used is grid interac-
tive inverter. When the power that is sold to the utility is sold at the same rate as the power 
that is bought from the utility, the term used is net metering. Some utilities have net metering 
agreements, and some buy power from the local producer at a different rate from what they 
charge for the grid power. In either case, the grid interactive inverter has to synchronize with 
the utility before the connection is completed so that the power supplied to the grid matches 
the grid requirements. If the utility system loses power, the premises generation cannot remain 
connected to continue supplying power to the utility grid. This is a dangerous situation, as util-
ity workers are working under the assumption that their system is deenergized, and they would

Micro-Hydro Generators
Another alternate source of electrical energy is the use of small electrical generators that use 
the force of flowing water to provide the mechanical energy needed to spin the rotor. The 
concept is to generate small amounts of power by directing water to these generators from 
moving streams, or even from moving water such as tidal pools, or from moving boats. These 
small generators are used to provide supplementary power to remote locations or additional 
power without the need for fuels. There are three basic types of turbines currently designed for 
different applications.
The impulse style uses a “spinner”-type propeller where the water is channeled to the 
propeller and strikes it to cause the mechanical force. This system works well if there is vertical 
drop, or “head,” of 20 feet or more, but requires little water volume. Another style of micro-
turbines is a reaction-style turbine. With this style, there must be a large flow of water directed 
to the impellor, but it does not need much head. It can be installed in a moving stream. The 
third style is a submersible impeller that takes advantage of a fast-moving stream or is used 
over the side of a moving boat. All these systems are designed to charge batteries at typically 
12 to 24 VDC. The batteries are part of a storage system that can receive power from multiple 
sources. The output is then connected to inverters to provide the system voltage desired.
UPS Using Flywheel Technology
Uninterruptible power supply (UPS) is used where there cannot be an interruption to the 
power supply. (See UPS battery systems in Unit 12.) At least one manufacturer has incor-
porated the physics of momentum into a power generation system. This system is called the 
battery-free uninterruptible power system (BF-UPS).
This alternative energy storage system uses the concept of stored energy in the inertia 
of a spinning flywheel. During normal power supply to a facility, the BF-UPS allows a large 
flywheel to spin at a constant speed. The flywheel is constantly spinning on high quality bear-
ing, so the energy needed to maintain momentum in the flywheel is minimal. In the event of a 
line disturbance or a power failure, the spinning flywheel gives up its energy to keep an integral 
generator running with no interruption of power. The flywheel is sized to provide up to 2 min-
utes of full power before the flywheel begins to slow down. This is usually enough time to start 
a standby generator and bring it up to synchronization speed to pick up the load. A side benefit 
of the flywheel system is that it can actually supply the DC starting power the generator needs, 
so there are no failed batteries for the load power or the generator starting system. There is no 
battery charging or battery maintenance, so the environmental considerations are eliminated. 
Because no batteries are required, much less floor space is dedicated to this type of UPS.

The drawback of the system is that it is usually sized to provide load power for a short duration, 
just enough to get a traditional generator operating.
The installation of this system has the same parameters as a normal standby/UPS system 
but without the need for batteries.
SEPARATELY DERIVED SYSTEMS
Although separately derived systems are not alternative energy, they are a separate source of 
power that is derived within a customer’s premises. Separately derived systems sometimes 
cause confusion when you are dealing with the rules and regulations of the National Electrical 
Code. The term is meant to indicate that a new source of electrical power is now available at a 
premises. The source of power can be a transformer that is located within a building or on the 
property of the building owner and is not a utility transformer. It can be a generator that is used 
for emergency stand-by, or extra power that can be connected to the buildings electrical system.
A definition paraphrased from the NEC indicates that it is a premises wiring system whose 
power is derived from a source other than the electric service. The separately derived system 
shall have no direct (electrical) connection from the circuit conductors from one system to the 
circuit conductors of another system, including the neutral conductor. Only the connections 
that are permitted are the connections through the earth, metal enclosures, metal raceways, or 
equipment grounding conductors.
This definition determines the connections made and the requirements for grounding and 
bonding of the separate systems. The idea that a transformer within a building is actually a sepa-
rate source of power to the transformer’s load is the key to this concept. The power from the 
secondary of this transformer is derived from the primary but is through magnetic circuitry and 
not through electrical connections. Therefore, the secondary of the transformer has no electri-
cal references for phase or neutrals, as does the primary. It is a separate electrical system only 
linked through magnetics in the transformer. See Figure 15–13 for an isolation  transformer. 
A style of transformer connection that does not provide this isolation and is not a separately 
derived system is an autotransformer used to step up or step down a voltage for small varia-
tions. A transformer used to step a voltage from 208 to 
230 volts often uses a connection called a boost auto-
transformer. In this pattern, the circuit conductors, 
especially the grounded neutral conductor, is connected 
between the primary and the secondary, and therefore 
this does not fit the definition of a separately derived 
system. See Figure 15–14 for an autotransformer con-
nection. Once you have decided that the transformer 
is a separate system, then the secondary connections 
to feeders and branch circuits are treated in much the 
same way as a new service within the building.

SUMMARY
Besides the usual sources of electrical power from the utility power grid, there are alternate 
sources for spot supply or remote supply of electricity. UPS battery systems and engine-driven 
generating sets were discussed in Unit 12, but other unique systems are now becoming more 
popular and are more cost effective than before. Each system has its own benefits and drawbacks, 
but each of these alternative systems other than microturbines, does not burn fossil fuels or  create 
the emission problems of other fuels. Nuclear fuel does not create the air pollution problems, but 
has a lot of other inherent problems. Hydroelectric generation has a different set of problems, 
such as it can only be produced with the correct topographical conditions. Because these alter-
native sources are small-scale production, they are typically for local use. However, wind power 
can be used for small-scale electrical generation or for large-scale utility grade generation and can 
be tied to the power grid to help meet the need for power production on a regional basis. Solar 
power is also a production technique that can be used for local small-scale production; it is cov-
ered by the NEC for premises wiring and is also being used as a large-scale production technique 
on solar farms.

BJECTIVES
After studying this unit, the student should be able to
• explain how and why transformers are used for the  
transmission and distribution of electrical energy.
• describe the basic construction of a transformer.
• distinguish between the primary and secondary 
windings of a transformer.
• list, in order of sequence, the various steps in the  
operation of a step-up transformer.
• make use of appropriate information to calculate the  
voltage ratio, voltages, currents, and efficiency for  
step-up and step-down transformers.
• explain how the primary load changes with the  
 secondary load.

TRANSFORMER 
BASICS 17

U N I T  1 7   T R A N S F O R M E R  B A S I C S
162
Large amounts of alternating-current energy may be generated at a convenient voltage 
using steam, nuclear, winf or water power. Transformers are used first to increase this energy 
to a high voltage for transmission over many miles of transmission wires, and then to decrease 
this voltage to values that are conve-
nient and safe for use by the consumer.
ELEMENTS OF 
TRANSFORMERS
A transformer consists of two or 
more conductor windings placed on 
the same iron core magnetic path, as 
shown in Figure 17–2.
Laminated Core
The iron core of a transformer is made 
up of sheets of rolled iron. This iron is 
treated so that it has a high magnetic 
conducting quality (high permeabil-
ity) throughout the length of the core. 
It is neither efficient nor economically feasible to generate large quantities of direct-current 
electrical energy. The invention of the transformer was a milestone in the development of 
the electrical industry. The transformer increases or decreases the voltage of large quantities 
of alternating-current energy efficiently, safely, and conveniently. A large power distribution 
 station is shown in Figure 17–1.
FIGURE 17–1 Substation with three oil-filled circuit breakers.

163
Windings
A basic transformer has two windings: the primary winding and the secondary winding. The 
primary winding is the coil that receives the energy. It is formed, wound, and fitted over the iron 
core. The secondary winding is the coil that provides the energy from the transformer to the load 
at a transformed or changed (increased or decreased) voltage.
Transformers by definition are used to transfer (transform) energy from one AC system 
to another by electromagnetic means. They do not change the amount of power significantly; 
only minor wattage losses occur in the transformer. If the transformer increases the voltage 
from primary to secondary, it is called a step-up transformer. If it decreases the voltage from 
primary to secondary, it is called a step-down transformer. Some transformers do not change the 
voltage level but only isolate the primary power source from the secondary power source. This 
type of transformer is known as an isolation transformer.
The secondary voltage is dependent on the following:
• voltage of the primary
• number of turns of the transformer looped wire on the primary winding
• number of turns of the transformer looped wire on the secondary winding
Permeability describes the ease with which a material conducts magnetic lines of force. The 
iron also has a high ohmic resistance across the plates (through the thickness of the core). It is 
necessary to laminate the iron sheets (Figure 17–3) to reduce hysteresis and eddy currents that 
cause heating of the core.
FIGURE 17–3 “E” lamination used in transformer core 
construction.
© Cengage Learning 2014

Certain core-type transformers have the primary and secondary wire coils wound on sep-
arate legs of the core [see Figure 17–2(A)]. The primary and secondary wire coils can also be 
wound on top of one another, as shown in Figure 17–2(B). Winding in this manner improves 
transformer efficiency and conserves energy. When stating the transformer ratio, the primary is 
the first factor of the ratio. This indicates which winding, high or low, is connected to the power 
source. A transformer that has twice as many primary winding loops compared to the number 
of secondary windings has a 2:1 ratio.
CONSTRUCTION OF TRANSFORMERS
As shown in Figure 17–4, three major types of construction for transformer cores are core, 
shell, and cross (or H).
Core Type
In a core transformer, the primary winding is on one leg of the transformer and the secondary 
winding is on the other leg. A more efficient type of core construction is the shell, in which the 
core is surrounded by a shell of iron [Figure 17–4(A) and (B)].

Shell Type
The shell or double-window core transformer [Figure 17–4(C)] is probably used most fre-
quently in electrical work. In terms of energy conservation, this transformer design operates at 
98% or higher efficiency.
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has

165
Cross, or H, Type
The cross, or H, core is also called the modified shell type [Figure 17–4(D)]. The coils are sur-
rounded by four core legs. The cross type is really a combination of two shell cores set at right 
angles to each other. The windings are located over the center core, which is four times the area of 
each of the outside legs. This type of core is very compact and can be cooled easily. It is used for 
large power transformers where voltage drop and cost must be kept to a minimum. These units 
are usually immersed in oil for high-insulation properties and effective cooling. Another method 
of cooling the transformers is by forced air. Transformers should never be immersed in water 
for cooling. Accidental flooding, such as in underground transformer vaults, should be pumped.
ELEMENTARY PRINCIPLES OF TRANSFORMER OPERATION
According to Lenz’s law, a voltage is induced in a coil whenever the coil current is increased or 
decreased. This induced voltage is always in such a direction as to oppose the force  producing 
it. Called induction, this action is illustrated by arranging two loops of wire, as shown in 
Figure 17–5.
Note in Figure 17–5 the progressive enlargement of the magnetic field about one side of 
each loop as the current builds up. The strength of the magnetic field increases as the electrical 
current through the conductor increases from the power source. Figure 17–5 also shows the 
field pattern during the period that the current decreases.

Figure 17–5 uses the left-hand rule for con-
ductors. Grasp the conductor in your left hand 
with your thumb extended in the direction of the 
electron flow. Your fingers indicate the direction 
of the magnetic flux. The flux expands outward 
from the conductor as the current flow increases, 
and contracts toward the conductor center as the 
current flow diminishes.
As the current builds up to its maximum value, 
the circular magnetic lines around the wire move 
outward from the wire. This outward movement of 
magnetic lines of force cuts across the conductor of 
the second loop. As a result, an EMF is induced and 
current circulates in the loop, as indicated on the 
galvanometer located above the conductor.
When the current reaches its steady state in 
the first circuit, the flux is stationary, and no volt-
age is induced in the circuit. The galvanometer 
indicates zero current.
When the battery circuit is opened, current 
falls to zero and the flux collapses. The collapsing 
flux cuts through the second circuit and again 
induces an EMF. The second induced current has a direction opposite to that of the first 
induced current, as indicated by the galvanometer needle. The final stage shows a steady state 
with no field and no induced current. This action is automatic with AC applied.
The loops of wire may be replaced by two concentric coils (loops with many turns) to 
form a transformer. Figure 17–6 shows a transformer that has a primary winding, an iron core, 
and a secondary winding. When a changing or alternating current is delivered to the primary 
winding, the changing primary current produces a changing magnetic field in the iron core. 
This changing field cuts through the secondary coil and thus induces a voltage; the value 
depends on the number of conductors in the secondary coil cut by the magnetic lines. This 
is called mutual inductance. Commercial transformers generally have fixed cores that provide 
complete magnetic circuits for efficient operation where there is little flux leakage and high 
mutual induction.
VOLTAGE RATIO
According to Lenz’s law, 1 volt is induced when 100,000,000 magnetic lines of force are cut 
in 1 second. The primary winding of a transformer supplies the magnetic field for the core. 
The secondary winding, when placed directly over the same core, supplies the load with an

the impressed voltage.) Because the secondary winding is on the same core as the primary 
winding, only 50 volts is induced in the secondary, as only half as many conductors are cut 
by the magnetic field.

CURRENT RATIO
The current ratio in a transformer is the inverse of the ratio for voltage transformation. The 
transformer does not create power and is not designed to consume power. The input power 
should be very close to the output power. Therefore, if the volt-amps input equals the volt-
amps output and the voltage level must be increased, the current level must be decreased. The 
voltage ratio and the current ratio are inversely proportional.
If the load current of the transformer shown in Figure 17–7 is 12 amperes, the primary 
current must be such that the product of the number of turns and the value of the current 
(ampere-turns primary) equals the value of the ampere-turns secondary.

Ep × Ip = Es × Is ; 120 V × 48 A = 5670 VA ; 480 V × 12 A = 5670 VA
The current ratio is an inverse ratio; that is, the greater the number of turns, the less 
the current for a given load. Practical estimates of primary or secondary currents are made by 
assuming that transformers are 100% efficient.

p
The greater the current, the larger the size of the wire leads on the transformer. From this 
information, you can usually determine the high- and low-voltage sides.
Higher voltage = lower current, and therefore smaller wire size
Lower voltage = higher current, and therefore larger wire size
● Example:  A machine tool being relocated has a disconnected control transformer. The 
nameplate is illegible due to corrosion. The motor power circuit is 480 volts. 
The motor controller operates on 120 volts of control. Which is the primary 
and secondary of the control transformer? The higher voltage has the smaller 
wire size. Therefore, the smaller wire leads are to be connected to the 480 volts.
The use of an ohmmeter can also tell you which winding has the greater resistance. By 
measuring each winding, you find that the greater the resistance, the higher the voltage connec-
tion, because it has more turns of smaller wire. Remember, primary refers to the supply side of 
the transformer. Secondary refers to the load side (Figure 17–8).

SCHEMATIC DIAGRAM OR SYMBOL
A step-up transformer is usually shown in schematic form, as illustrated in Figure 17–8. The 
ratio of turns, primary to secondary, is not usually listed. This figure could be a step-up or step-
down symbol representation.
PRIMARY LOADING WITH SECONDARY LOADING
The current in the secondary controls the current in the primary. When the secondary circuit 
is complete by placing a load across it, the secondary EMF causes a current to flow. This builds 
up a magnetic field in opposition to the primary field. This opposing, or demagnetizing, action 
reduces the effective field of the primary flux, which in turn reduces the primary CEMF, thereby 
permitting current to flow in the primary. The greater the current flow in the secondary, the 
greater the field produced by the secondary. This results in a reduced primary field; hence, a 
reduced primary CEMF is produced. This condition permits greater current flow in the pri-
mary. This entire process repeats itself whenever there is any change in the value of the current 
in the secondary circuit. A transformer adjusts itself readily to any normal change in secondary 
load. However, if a direct short is placed across the secondary, the abnormally large amount of 
current flowing causes the primary current to rise in a like manner, resulting in damage to, or 
complete burn-out of, the transformer if it is not protected properly.
EFFICIENCY
The efficiency of all machinery is the ratio of the output to the input:

In general, transformer efficiency is about 97%. Only 3% of the total wattage at the 
 secondary winding is lost through the transformation. The loss in wattage is due to core losses 
and copper losses.
The core loss is the result of hysteresis (magnetic friction) and eddy currents (induced 
currents) in the iron core.
The copper loss is power lost in the copper wire of the windings (I2R). Therefore, taking 
these losses into consideration,
=
× 100
% Efficiency
Watts input (primary)
Watts output (secondary)
where Watts input = Watts output + losses.
SUMMARY
Transformers are very useful in delivering the exact voltage needed by customers. DC cannot be 
easily changed from one voltage level to another. There are no true DC transformers. AC can 
be increased or decreased easily through the electromagnetic coupling of the transformer coils. 
Transformers can be used to (1) step up the voltage, (2) step down the voltage, or (3) simply 
isolate the transformer primary system from the transformer secondary system.

A single-phase transformer usually has a core and at least two coils. The single-phase auto-
transformer has only one coil. The specifications for single-phase transformers vary greatly, and 
the applications of these transformers are unlimited.
THE DOUBLE-WOUND TRANSFORMER (ISOLATING 
AND INSULATING)
The double-wound transformer has a primary winding and a secondary winding. These wind-
ings are independently isolated and insulated from each other. A shielded winding transformer, on 
the other hand, is designed with a metallic shield between the primary and secondary windings, 
providing a safety factor by grounding. This prevents accidental contact between the windings 
under fault conditions. The illustrations in Unit 17 show a double-wound transformer. The coils 
of double-wound transformers may be connected in several different arrangements.
Figure 18–1 shows popular arrangements of single-phase transformer windings. Shown 
in Figure 18–1(A), two single coils are used for specific step-down or step-up applications, 
including bell-ringing transformers, neon transformers, and component transformers for com-
mercial equipment, such as automatic machines, switchgear, and other devices. Multiple coil 
primary and secondary windings, shown in Figure 18–1(B), are used in distribution transform-
ers where dual voltage ratings are desired. Arrangements for voltage ratings of 2400//120/240 
or 240/480//120/240 are common.
FIGURE 18–1 Coil arrangements for single-phase transformers.

Double-wound transformers separate or insulate the high transmission voltages from the 
typical consumer voltages of 115/230/460. The NEC requires this type of transformer in all 
distribution circuits except those circuits assigned to autotransformers. Here, as in the Code, 
the voltage considered shall be that at which the circuit operates, except for the examples given.
Polarity
A 460/115/230 transformer may be connected for two ratios: 460/115 or 460/230. To 
obtain the 460/115 ratio, the secondary coils are connected in parallel; the 460/230 ratio

The beginning or ending of a transformer coil is usually indicated by a tab placed on the 
lead by the technician in charge of the winding process. When the transformer is assembled, 
other markings often replace the original ones. Before final inspection, a polarity test must be 
made to be certain that the leads are marked correctly.
IDENTIFYING AN UNMARKED TRANSFORMER
Installed transformers often have missing or disfigured tabs. Every time a transformer is to be 
reconnected following repairs or for other reasons, the polarity of the leads must be checked.
Figures 18–3 and 18–4 illustrate two systems of marking polarity. In conventional usage, 
polarity refers to the induced voltage vector relationships of the transformer leads as they are 
brought outside of the tank. The American National Standards Institute (ANSI) standardized 
the location of these leads to obtain additive and subtractive polarity conditions. All high-
voltage leads brought outside the case are marked H1, H2, and so forth, whereas the low-voltage 
leads are marked X1 and X2. The H1 lead is located on the left side when facing the low-voltage 
leads. H1 and X1 have the same electrical polarity at the same instant of time.
Subtractive Polarity. The tank diagram in Figure 18–3 shows the relationship of the 
induced voltages in the primary and secondary windings for the subtractive polarity condition. 
Transformers connected in this manner have the H1 and X1 leads located directly opposite 
each other. If H1 and X1 are connected together [as shown in Figure 18–5(A)], the voltage 
measured between H2 and X2 is less than the primary voltage. The induced voltage opposes 
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

 
U N I T  1 8   S I N G L E - P H A S E  T R A N S F O R M E R S
178
the supply voltage and thus causes the secondary induced voltage to be subtracted from the 
primary voltage.
Additive Polarity. The tank diagram in Figure 18–4 shows the voltage relationship of 
the induced voltages for the additive polarity connection. When H1 and X2 are connected, the 
voltage across H2 and X1 is greater than the primary voltage. The measured voltages add up to 
the sum of the primary and secondary voltages, as shown in Figure 18–5(B).
Transformers that are rated up to 200 kVA (kilovolt-amperes) and have the value of 
the high-voltage winding equal to 8660 volts or less are additive. All other transformers are 
subtractive.
Test for P olarity. Transformer coils often must be connected in series or parallel, as 
in Figure 18–6. For these situations, the polarity of a transformer or any secondary coil can 
be found by making the connections shown in Figure 18–5(A) and (B). Connect the adja-
cent left, high- and low-voltage outlet leads facing the low-voltage side of the transformer.

Apply a low-voltage supply to the primary and note the voltage between the adjacent right-
hand, high- and low-voltage terminals.
• For subtractive polarity, the voltmeter reading (V) is less than the applied voltage. 
The voltage is the difference between the primary and secondary voltages, Ep − Es 
[Figure 18–5(A)].
• For additive polarity, the voltmeter reading (V) is greater than the applied voltage. The 
voltage is the sum of the primary and secondary voltages, Ep + Es [Figure 18–5(B)].
If the test shown in Figure 18–5(B) indicates additive polarity, the secondary leads inside 
the tank must be reversed at the bottom of the bushings to obtain a true subtractive polarity. 
If the transformer requires all additive polarity and the test indicates subtractive, reverse the 
secondary lead markers so that X2 is located opposite H1.

In all transformers, the H terminals are always the high-voltage terminals. The X termi-
nals are always the low-voltage terminals. Either the H or X terminals can be designated as the 
primary or the secondary, depending on which is the source and which is the load and whether 
the transformer is used as a step-up or step-down transformer.
SINGLE-PHASE TRANSFORMER CONNECTIONS
Series Connection
If a 460/115/230-volt, single-phase transformer is to be connected to obtain 460/230 volts, the 
two secondary coils must be connected in series. The beginning and ending of each coil must be 
joined, as shown in Figure 18–6(A). The “start” of each coil is identified by an odd-numbered 
subscript.
Note: If the voltage is zero across X1−X4 after the series connections are complete, the 
coils are opposing each other (the polarity of one coil is reversed). To correct this situation, 
reverse one coil, and then reconnect and recheck the polarity.
Parallel Connection
To obtain 460/115 volts, the two secondary coils must be connected in parallel, as shown in 
Figure 18–6(B). The polarity of each coil must be correct before making this connection. The 
parallel connection of two coils of opposite polarity results in a short circuit and internal dam-
age to the transformer.

Note: An indirect polarity check can be made by completing the series connection and 
noting the total voltage. As noted previously, zero voltage indicates opposite polarities. Reverse 
one coil to remedy the condition and then recheck overall polarity. Retag leads if necessary.
PARALLEL OPERATION OF SINGLE-PHASE TRANSFORMERS
Single-phase distribution transformers can be connected in parallel only if the voltage and 
percent impedance ratings of the transformers are identical. This information is found on the 
nameplates of large transformers. It is recommended that this rule be followed when making 
permanent parallel connections of all transformers.
TRANSFORMER PRIMARY TAPS
Taps are nothing more than alternative terminals that can be connected to more closely match 
the primary supply voltage. These taps are arranged in increments of 2.5% or 5% of the primary 
nominal voltage rating of the transformer (Figure 18–7). This provides a job-site adjustment to 
ensure that the transformer primary matches the supply voltage. The secondary then produces 
the desired secondary voltage.
FIGURE 18–7 Primary terminal taps.

The voltage received from the power utility may be low or high. Because the transformer 
is a fixed voltage device, the output voltage is always in direct proportion to the input voltage. If 
the ratio is 2:1 and the supply voltage is 480 volts, the output is 240 volts. If the primary voltage 
is 438 volts, the secondary is only 219 volts.
High and low voltages can have serious effects on different connected loads. Therefore, 
care must be taken to deliver a voltage as close as possible to the desired primary so that the 
 secondary voltage matches the equipment nameplate voltages. Consistently high and low

 voltage problems can be solved by connecting the proper primary taps (Figure 18–8). If the 
voltage fluctuates consistently, tap changing is not the solution. A voltage-regulating trans-
former is needed.
REGULATION
A slight voltage drop at the secondary terminals from no load to full load, called regulation, is 
caused by resistance and reactance drops in the windings. Regulation is expressed as a percentage. 
Regulation of constant potential transformers is about 1–5% at the secondary terminals, as follows:

AUTOTRANSFORMER
Transformers with only one winding are called autotransformers. In an autotransformer, a part 
of the winding is common to both primary and secondary circuits. This is the most efficient 
type of transformer because a portion of one winding carries the difference between the 
primary and secondary currents. Figure 18–9 shows the current distribution in an autotrans-
former used in a typical lighting application. The disadvantage of an autotransformer is the fact 
that the use of only one winding makes it impossible to insulate the low-voltage section from 
the high-voltage distribution line. If the low-voltage winding opens when stepping down the 
voltage, the full-line voltage appears across the load. Autotransformers are used in some motor 
starters to reduce current draw from the line. See Figure 18–10 for an autotransformer motor 
starter.
FIGURE 18–9 Autotransformer used for lighting.

According to the 2011 National Electrical Code® Article 450, autotransformers rated 
at 600 volts or less shall have an individual overcurrent device connected in series with the 
ungrounded input conductor. If the primary current is less than 9 amps, the overcurrent rating 
shall not be more than 125% of the rated primary current. If the primary current is 9 amps or 
more, the overcurrent value can be the next standard larger size over 125%. An overcurrent 
device shall not be installed in series with the shunt winding (common winding to primary 
and secondary), with one exception noted.

DRY AND LIQUID-FILLED TRANSFORMERS
Dry transformers are used extensively for indoor installations. These transformers are cooled 
by air and are not encased in heavy tanks such as those required for liquid-filled transformers. 
Dry transformers are used for bell-ringing circuits, current and potential transformers, welding 
transformers, and almost all transformers used on portable or small industrial equipment, or for 
separately derived systems within a facility.
Liquid-filled transformers consist of the core and coils immersed in a tank of oil or other 
insulating liquid. Oil cooling is approximately 15 times more effective than air cooling. Many 
distribution transformers designed for outdoor installation are liquid filled.
METHODS OF COOLING
The method selected to cool a transformer must not only maintain a sufficiently low average 
temperature but must also prevent an excessive temperature rise in any portion of the trans-
formers. In other words, the cooling medium must prevent the formation of “hot spots.” For this 
FIGURE 18–10 Autotransformer used for 
motor-starting duty.
© Cengage Learning 2014
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

U N I T  1 8   S I N G L E - P H A S E  T R A N S F O R M E R S  
185
reason, the working parts of the transformer are usually immersed in a high grade of insulating 
oil. Because the oil must be free of any moisture, the oil must be filtered to remove moisture as 
necessary. The electrical insulating value of the oil is checked periodically.
Duct lines are arranged within the transformer to provide for the free circulation of oil 
through the core and coils. The warmer and thus lighter oil rises to the top of the steel tank. 
The transformer core and windings are placed near the bottom of the tank. The cooler and 
heavier oil settles to the bottom of the tank. This natural circulation provides for better cooling 
(Figure 18–11).
FIGURE 18–11 An oil-filled power transformer with radiators.
© Cengage Learning 2014
Forced Cooling
Several methods of removing heat from a transformer involve forced cooling. Cooling is 
achieved by using pumps to force the circulation of the oil or liquid by forcing the circulation 
of air past the oil-filled radiators (Figure 18–12) or by immersing water-containing coils in the 
oil. Cold water circulating in the coils removes the heat stored in the oil. Figure 18–13 shows 
internal construction of an oil-filled transformer. Forced air movement by the use of fans is a 
common practice. Fans are generally controlled by thermostats (Figure 18–14).
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

APPLICATION
Single-phase transformers are suitable for use in a wide variety of applications, as shown by the 
examples in Figures 18–15, 18–16, 18–17, and 18–18.
Distribution transformers are usually oil filled and mounted on poles, in vaults, or in 
manholes.
Compensator starters are tapped autotransformers used for starting induction motors.
Instrument transformers, such as potential and current transformers, are made in indoor, 
outdoor, and portable styles used for metering.
Welding transformers provide a very low voltage to arc welding electrodes. Movable 
 secondaries provide the varying voltage and current characteristics required.
Constant-current transformers are used for series street lighting where the current must be 
held constant with a varying voltage. The secondary is movable.

SAFETY PRECAUTIONS
Although there are no moving parts in a transformer, some maintenance procedures must be 
performed. For a general overhaul of an operating transformer or when an internal inspection 
is to be made, the transformer must be deenergized. Do not assume that the transformer is 
 disconnected or rely on someone else to disconnect it; always check the transformer yourself. 
You must be sure that the fuses are pulled open or out and that the switch or circuit breaker is 
open on both the primary and secondary sides. After the transformer is disconnected, the wind-
ings should be grounded to discharge any capacitive energy stored in the equipment. This step 
also protects you while you are at work. Grounding is accomplished with a device commonly 
known as a “short and ground.” This is a flexible cable with clamps on both ends. The ground 
end is clamped first, and then, using a hook stick, the other end is touched to the conductor. 
Do this with each leg on the primary and secondary sides. The phases are then shorted together 
and grounded for your protection.
The tank pressure should be relieved. This may be done by bleeding a valve or plug above 
the oil level. Any gas in the tank must be replaced with fresh air before a person enters the tank. 
The absence of oxygen in a tank causes asphyxiation quickly and without warning. A second 
person should be on duty outside the transformer as a safety precaution whenever someone 
must enter the transformer. Be sure to follow Occupational Safety and Health Administration 
(OSHA) rules on hazardous entry and restricted work space. All tools should have safety 
cords attached, with the other end tightly secured. All pockets in clothing should be emptied. 
Nothing must be allowed to fall into the tank. Great care must be exercised to prevent contact-
ing or coming close to the electrical conductors and other live parts of the transformer unless 
it is known that the transformer has been deenergized. The tank and cooling radiators should 
not be touched until it is determined that they are adequately grounded (for both new and old 
installations).
SUMMARY
Single-phase transformers are used in a variety of applications. To connect them correctly, the 
electrician must know how the AC polarities are established at the transformer leads. Because 
the internal windings of the transformer are often not accessible, polarity checks must be made 
on the external leads. The transformer leads are marked according to the standards for additive 
or subtractive polarity. If the lead markings are not apparent or the voltages expected are not 
obtained, then the polarity must be tested. If the lead markings are known, the transformer coils 
may be connected in series or parallel to yield the desired voltage. Autotransformer connections 
or tap-changing transformers may be used to bring the voltage within the desired range. Much 
of a transformer’s capability to operate satisfactorily depends on the capability to dissipate the 
heat produced in the windings. Many methods are used to dissipate this heat and keep the trans-
former from overheating and being destroyed.

UNIT
SINGLE-PHASE, 
THREE-WIRE 
SECONDARY SYSTEM
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has

U N I T  1 9   S I N G L E - P H A S E ,  T H R E E - W I R E  S E C O N D A R Y  S Y S T E M
Most homes are wired for three-wire service. Because electric ranges and clothes dryers 
are designed for three-wire operation, any home to be provided with these appliances must 
have three-wire service. The three wires terminate in the residence at the load center panel so 
that most individual circuits carried through the house are at 120 volts, thus eliminating the 
dangers with 240-volt circuits.
The double-wound transformer is used as the source for three-wire secondary distribu-
tion. One of the important advantages of a transformer is its capability to provide a three-wire 
circuit from the low-voltage secondary. A step-down transformer with a 2400/240/120-volt 
rating is commonly used in residential installations.
The advantages of using three-wire service in general distribution systems include (1) a 
reduction in the cost of main feeders and subfeeders, (2) the provision of 120-volt service for 
normal lighting circuits and 240-volt service for power and motor loads, and (3) the conserva-
tion of electrical energy by reducing wattage loss in transmission.
Figure 19–1 is a schematic of a typical three-wire system. The secondary coils are connected 
in series, and each coil is rated at 120 volts. The junction N between the two secondary coils is 
usually grounded. This precaution provides some protection to a person who may come into 
contact accidentally with a transformer that has faulty insulation. The line wire carried from this 
junction (N) to the several loads is known as the neutral or grounded conductor. The neutral wire 
generally carries less current than wires L1 and L2, except when the load is on one side only, that 
is, L1 to N or L2 to N. The 240-volt motor load does not affect the current flowing in the neutral 
wire. The neutral is carried through the system as an unfused or switched conductor. If the neutral 
opens, and the loads in the 120-volt circuits are greatly unbalanced, then these 120-volt circuits 
are subjected to approximately 240 volts. The neutral is designed to carry not only the unbalanced 
current in the two 120-volt circuits, but also the entire load on any one side should all the load 
on the other side be cut off completely. This latter situation can occur if a fuse or circuit breaker 
suddenly opens either line. Figure 19–1 shows the current distribution for the loads indicated.
FIGURE 19–1 Schematic diagram of a three-wire system supplied from a single-phase

OPEN NEUTRAL
As an example to show what occurs when the neutral of a three-wire system opens, assume that 
the lighting load in Figure 19–1 is a pure resistive load.
Thus, the group of four lamps has a resistance of  
=

With the neutral open, these two groups combine as a series circuit with a resistance of 
12.8 ohms connected across 240 volts. The current flow through this series circuit follows:
=
= 18.75 amperes

Then, according to the laws for a series circuit, the voltage across the 8-ohm group (two 
lamps) is equal to
I × R = 18.75 × 8 = 150 volts
and the voltage across the 4.6-ohm group (four lamps) is equal to
I × R = 18.75 × 4.8 = 90 volts
(Remember that in a series circuit, the highest voltage appears across the highest value of 
resistance.) The two lamps would probably burn out with this open neutral.

U N I T  1 9   S I N G L E - P H A S E ,  T H R E E - W I R E  S E C O N D A R Y  S Y S T E M  
ECONOMICS OF THE THREE-WIRE SYSTEM 
FOR FEEDERS AND BRANCH CIRCUITS
Using the three-wire system of the previous problem as an example, the total load transmitted 
over the three wires is 1500 W, or 1.5 kW, at a power factor of 100%. It is assumed that the 
motor load is provided with power factor correction. If single conductor-type TW wire is used 
from the transformer to the load, the following sizes are required:
Line 1 (55 amperes): No. 6 TW
Neutral (0.70 × 55 = 38.5): No. 8 TW
Line 2 (55 amperes): No. 6 TW
Although No. 8 TW wire is the actual size permitted for the neutral, a substitution of a 
No. 6 TW wire can be made so that three 6 lines are provided to simplify the installation.
If a two-wire distribution system is used for the same load, the total current is 
11,500/120  = 96 amperes. Two AWG 1 lines are required. If the transmission distance is 
100 feet, then a comparison can be made of the weights of copper wire required for the two 
systems.
Three-Wire System
For a No. 6 TW line, the weight per 100 feet = 11.5 lb. Therefore, for three No. 6 TW lines, 
the total weight = 3 × 11.5 = 34.5 lb.
Two-Wire System
For a TW 1 line, the weight per 100 feet = 33 lb. For two 1 lines, the total weight = 2 × 33 = 
66 lb. Therefore, for the same load, the three-wire system uses less copper (66 − 34.5 = 
31.5 lb less) than the two-wire system.
A similar conclusion can be reached by consulting a manufacturer’s price list and noting 
the lower prices for smaller conductors. The copper losses in the line are also considerably less 
for a three-wire system for several reasons: the motor power is transmitted at a higher voltage 
requiring less current for a given load; the neutral carries no current when the two lighting 
circuits are balanced; and the copper losses are much less because less wire and current are 
required. These line losses are of two types: (1) voltage drop (IR) and (2) wattage loss (I2R).
SUMMARY
The three-wire, single-phase system is the most common residential electrical service. The 
three wires constitute a single phase of AC delivered to the home. The transformer secondary 
coil is tapped at the center point and grounded at that point to establish a ground reference. 
The power can then be divided into two 120-volt supplies and can also be used as a 240-volt 
single phase for higher power consumption appliances. Care must be taken to solidly ground 
the neutral center-tapped point because an open neutral conductor can cause severe damage.

TRANSFORMERS 
CONNECTED IN DELTA 
AND OPEN DELTA
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has

U N I T  2 0   T R A N S F O R M E R S  C O N N E C T E D  I N  D E LTA  A N D  O P E N  D E LTA
202
Most electrical energy is generated by three-phase, alternating-current generators. Three-
phase systems are used to transmit and distribute the generated electrical energy. The voltage 
on three-phase systems often must be transformed, either from a higher value to a lower value 
or from a lower value to a higher value.
Voltage transformation on three-phase systems can be obtained with the use of three 
single-phase transformers (Figure 20–1). These transformers can be connected in several ways 
to obtain the desired voltage values.
A possible connection pattern that the electrician is often required to use for the three 
single-phase transformers is the closed-delta connection.
Another connection pattern that is commonly used is the open-delta, or V, connection 
that requires only two transformers to transform voltage on a three-phase system.
CLOSED-DELTA CONNECTION
When three single-phase coils are connected so that each coil end is connected to the beginning 
of another coil, a simple closed-delta system is formed (Figure 20–2).
FIGURE 20–1 Three large single-phase, station-class, 
oil-filled power transformers.
Courtesy of Pennsylvania Transformer Technology, Inc.

deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

When the three coils are marked Coil A, Coil B, and Coil C, the end of each of the three 
coils is marked with the letter O. The beginnings of the coils are marked A, B, and C. Note that 
each coil end is connected to another coil beginning. Each of the three junction points ties to a 
line lead feeding a three-phase system.
If three single-phase transformers are to be used to step down 2400 volts, three phase to 
240 volts, three phase, a closed-delta connection is used. Each of the three transformers is rated 
at 2400 volts on the high-voltage side and 240 volts on the low-voltage side (Figure 20–3).

CONNECTING THE DELTA
The transformer leads on the high-voltage side of each single-phase transformer are marked 
H1 and H2. The leads on the low-voltage side of each single-phase transformer are marked X1 
and X2.
To connect the high-voltage primary windings in the closed-delta pattern to a three-phase 
source, the three windings are connected as follows: In making the connection, the end of one 
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has

primary winding is connected to the beginning of the next primary winding. In Figure 20–3, H1 
is the beginning of each coil and H2 is the end of each coil. Thus, each primary winding end H2 
is connected to the beginning H1 of another primary winding. A three-phase line wire is also 
connected at each junction point H1–H2. Note that the primary winding of each transformer is 
connected directly across the line voltage. This means that delta-connected transformers must 
be wound for the full-line voltage. In Figure 20–3, each of the three line voltages is 2400 volts, 
and the primary winding of each transformer is also rated at 2400 volts. After the high-voltage 
primary connections are made, the three-phase, 2400-volt input may be energized. It is not 
necessary to make polarity tests on the input side.
The next step is to connect the low-voltage output or secondary windings in the closed-
delta pattern. The secondary winding leads are marked X1 for the beginning of each coil and X2 
for the end of each coil. In making the connections on the secondary, the following procedure 
must be followed:
 
1. Check to see that the voltage output of each of the three transformers is 240 volts.
 
2.  With primary power off, connect the end of one secondary winding with the 
beginning of another secondary winding (Figure 20–4).

Caution: Never complete the last connection if there is a voltage difference greater than 
zero. If the connections are correct, this potential difference is zero. Observe safety precautions. 
Deenergize the primary while making connections.
When three transformers are connected with their primary windings in delta and their 
secondary windings in delta, the total connection is called a delta-delta (Δ-Δ) connection. The 
first delta symbol indicates the connection method of the primary windings, and the second 
delta symbol shows how the secondary windings are connected. When two or three single-
phase transformers are used to step down or step up voltage on a three-phase system, the group 
is called a transformer bank.
Figure 20–8 is another way of showing the closed-delta connection first illustrated in 
Figure 20–3. By tracing through the connection, it can be seen that the high-voltage and low-
voltage windings are all connected in the closed-delta pattern. This type of transformer diagram 
is often used by the electrician.
VOLTAGE AND CURRENT
In any closed-delta transformer connection, two important facts must be kept in mind.
• The line voltage and the voltage across the transformer windings are the same. 
A study of any delta connection shows that each transformer coil is connected directly 
across two line leads; therefore, the line voltage and the transformer coil voltage must 
be the same.
• The line current is greater than the coil current in a delta-connected transformer bank. 
The line current is equal to 1.73 × coil current. A study of a closed-delta transformer 
connection shows that each line lead is fed by two transformer coil currents that are out 
of phase and thus cannot be added directly by simple arithmetic.
FIGURE 20–7 A voltmeter reading indicates 
incorrect connections.

In the arrangement shown in Figure 20–9, the coil current in each transformer second-
ary is 10 amperes. The line current, however, is 1.73 × 10, or 17.3 amperes. Because the coil 
currents are out of phase, the total current is not 10 + 10, or 20, amperes. Rather, the total 
current is a resultant current in a balanced closed-delta system and is equal to 1.73 × coil cur-
rent (1.73 equals the square root of 3).
Three single-phase transformers of the same kVA capacity are used in almost all delta-
delta connected transformer banks used to supply balanced three-phase industrial loads. For 
example, if the industrial load consists of three-phase motors, the current in each line wire is 
balanced. To determine the total kVA capacity of the entire delta-delta connected transformer 
bank, add the three transformer kVA ratings. Thus, if each transformer is rated at 50 kVA, the 
total three-phase kVA is 50 + 50 + 50 = 150 kVA.

POWER AND LIGHTING SERVICE FROM A DELTA-DELTA 
CONNECTED TRANSFORMER BANK
A delta-delta connected transformer bank, with one transformer secondary center tapped, may 
be used to feed two types of load: (1) a 240-volt, three-phase industrial power load and (2) a 
120/240-volt, single-phase, three-wire lighting load.
The single-phase transformer that is to supply the single-phase, three-wire lighting load 
is usually larger in size than the other two transformers in the bank. This takes care of the 
additional lighting load placed here. A tap must be brought out from the midpoint of the 240-
volt, low-voltage winding so that the 120/240-volt, single-phase, three-wire service can be 
obtained. Many transformers are designed with the low-voltage side consisting of two 120-volt 
windings. These windings can be connected in series for 240 volts, in parallel for 120 volts, or 
in series and center tapped, with the tap brought out to give 120/240-volt, three-wire, single-
phase power.
Figure 20–10 illustrates three single-phase transformers connected as a delta-delta trans-
former bank. Each transformer has two 120-volt, low-voltage windings. These 120-volt wind-
ings are connected in series to give a total output voltage of 240 volts for each transformer. 
The connection scheme for the high-voltage input or primary windings is closed delta. The 
low-voltage output or secondary windings are also connected in the closed-delta pattern to

The three-phase, 240-volt industrial power system is also connected to the transformer

ground (120 × 1.73 = 208). This situation is called the high phase, or the NEC refers to this 
conductor as the phase with the higher voltage to ground.
Article 110.15 of the NEC requires that the phase be marked orange in color. Article 
408.3(E) requires that the B phase be designated as the phase with the higher voltage to ground 
and be placed in the middle in a switchboard or panelboard unless this is an addition to an exist-
ing installation with other arrangements.

Caution: The high-phase situation can be a serious hazard to human life as well as to 
any 120-volt equipment connected improperly between the high phase and neutral. See NEC 
Article 408.3(F) for marking a panel with a high leg.
Note that for ungrounded circuits, the greatest voltage between the given conductor and 
any other conductor of the circuit is considered the voltage to ground.
OPEN-DELTA, OR V, CONNECTION
A three-phase transformation of energy is possible using only two transformers. This con-
nection arrangement is called the open-delta, or V, connection. The open-delta connection is 
often used in an emergency when one of the three transformers in a delta-delta bank becomes 
defective. When it is imperative that a consumer’s three-phase power supply be restored as 
soon as possible, the defective transformer can be cut out of service, using the open-delta 
arrangement.
The following example shows how the open-delta connection can be used in an emer-
gency. Three 50 kVA transformers, each rated at 2400 volts on the high-voltage winding and 
240 volts on the low-voltage winding, are connected in a delta-delta bank (Figure 20–12). 
This closed-delta bank is used to step down a 2400-volt, three-phase input to a 240-volt, three-
phase output to supply an industrial consumer. Suddenly, the three-phase power service is 
 interrupted because lightning strikes and damages one of the transformers. The service must 
be restored immediately. This situation is shown in Figure 20–13.
FIGURE 20–11 One line diagram of delta-delta transformer with three-
phase, four-wire secondary.

Although it appears that the 
total kVA of the open-delta bank 
should be two-thirds that of a closed-
delta bank, the actual kVA rating of 
an open-delta bank is only 58% of the 
capacity of a closed-delta bank. The 
reason for this is that the currents of 
the two transformers in the open-
delta connection are out of phase, 
resulting in the total available capac-
ity of the open-delta bank being only 
58% instead of 66.7%.
In the open-delta example, three 
50 kVA transformers are connected in 
a delta-delta bank. This gives a total kVA capacity of 50 + 50 + 50 = 150 kVA for the closed-
delta bank. When one transformer is disconnected, the transformer bank changes to an open-
delta configuration, and the total kVA capacity now is only 58% of the original closed-delta 
capacity.
150 × 0.58 = 87 kVA
In some situations, an open-delta bank of transformers is installed initially. The third 
transformer is added when the increase in industrial power load on the transformer bank war-
rants the addition. When the third transformer is added to the bank, a closed-delta bank is 
formed.

2. Multiply the total kVA value by 86.5%. This gives the total kVA capacity of the 
open-delta transformer bank.
100 × 86.5% = 87 kVA
Therefore, an open-delta bank has a kVA capacity of 58% of the capacity of a closed-
delta bank: 150 KVA × .58 is the same total KVA as 865 × 100 KVA. Both calculations yield 
approximately 87 KVA.
Another way to explain the reduced percentages of output kVA is to use the rated voltages 
and currents. In an open-delta pattern, there is no vector addition of the current at the junc-
tion point; the line current is equal to the coil current. Just as in the closed-delta pattern, the 
open-delta voltage at the lines is the same as the coil voltages. The results can be seen in the 
following example: If each of the transformers are rated at 50 kVA and the secondary voltages 
are 240 volts, then the coil current of each transformer is 50,000/240 = 208 A. In an open-
delta pattern, Line I equals Coil I and Line E equals Coil E. The three-phase capacity of two 
transformers connected open delta is
Line E × Line I × 1.73 = 86.5 kVA
240 V × 208 A × 1.73 = ~ 86.5 kVA
This is the same as 86.55% of the two kVA added. This is also the same as 58% of the original 
150 kVA or 1.73 times the single 50 kVA.
THREE-PHASE TRANSFORMERS WITH PRIMARY TAPS
Some plant distribution transformers are preassembled and wired at the factory into a three-
phase bank in a single enclosure or as a single unit. These assemblies consist of three single-
phase transformers in one enclosure, usually the dry, air-cooled type. Some have primary tap 
terminals so that the supply voltage can be matched more closely (Figure 20–15). The electri-
cian must make the adjustment on the job until the primary of the transformer matches the 
measured supply voltage. The secondary then produces the desired voltage to achieve a closer 
match of the equipment nameplate voltages. Utilities do not always supply the desired precise 
voltages. There may also be a voltage drop within the plant so that the utility supply voltage is 
not actually available at the transformer.
When using taps on a three-phase transformer or bank of transformers, it is important 
that the same taps on each of the three primaries be connected in the same position on each 
Copyright 2013 Cengage Learning. All Rights Reserved. May not be copied, scanned, or duplicated, in whole or in part. Due to electronic rights, some third party content may be suppressed from the eBook and/or eChapter(s). Editorial review has 
deemed that any suppressed content does not materially affect the overall learning experience. Cengage Learning reserves the right to remove additional content at any time if subsequent rights restrictions require it.

 
U N I T  2 0   T R A N S F O R M E R S  C O N N E C T E D  I N  D E LTA  A N D  O P E N  D E LTA
214
coil. (See the “Transformer Primary Taps” section in Unit 18.) The following problems may 
result if the taps are not connected properly:
• The output voltage on each of the three secondary voltages is not the same. This pro-
duces high unbalanced currents that causes overheating of induction motors.
• An undesirable circulating current creates a “false load” condition if the transformer is 
connected delta-delta.
Taps are used for consistently high or low voltages. They are not used with voltages that 
fluctuate or vary frequently.
SUMMARY
Single-phase transformers are often used to create various patterns to supply three-phase loads. 
One pattern is the closed-delta pattern. In this pattern, the line voltage is the same as the phase 
voltage, but the current on the lines is 1.73 times the coil current. Be sure the coil leads are 
marked correctly, and double-check the connection procedures before energizing the delta 
transformer bank.
Single-phase transformers can be connected in an open-delta pattern to provide a reduced 
capacity power supply to a system if one of the phase transformers fails. Single-phase trans-
formers connected in the closed- or the open-delta pattern do not need to be the same kVA 
rating. Often, one transformer is larger if the system is to supply three-phase delta and some

Voltage transformation on three-phase systems 
can also be accomplished using wye- connected, single-
phase transformers (Figure 21–1). To avoid errors when 
wye connecting single-phase transformers, a systematic 
 method of making the connections should be used. The 
electrician should know the basic voltage and current 
relationships common to this type of connection.
FUNDAMENTAL WYE CONNECTION
A simple wye system is formed by  arranging three 
 single-phase coils so that one end  of each coil is 
 connected at a common point (Figure  21–2). Note 
that when these connections are shown in a schematic 
diagram, they resemble the letter Y (written as “wye”). 
This configuration is also sometimes known as a “star” 
connection.
As an example, Figure 21–3 shows the wye-wye 
connection of three single-phase transformers to step 
down a three-phase input of 4152 volts to a three-phase 
output of 208 volts. Each transformer must be voltage 
rated for its applications. The H2 leads or primary wind-
ing ends of the transformer are connected together. The 
beginning or H1 lead of each transformer is connected to 
one of the three line leads.

Two of the primary windings are connected across each pair of line wires. Each trans-
former primary winding is rated at 2400 volts, and the actual voltage applied to each of these 
three windings is 2400 volts. Note that the potential across each pair of line leads is 4152 volts 
and not the 2400 + 2400 = 4800 volts that might be expected because of the connection of 
two coils (this voltage is referred to as a nominal 4160 V).
The value of 4152 volts arises from the fact that the voltage applied to each of the primary 
windings is out of phase with the voltages applied to the other primary windings. As a result, 
these winding voltages cannot be added directly to obtain the line voltage. Rather, the line 
voltage is equal to 1.73 × coil voltage. Therefore, for the input side of the transformer bank in 
Figure 21–3 where the voltage on the primary winding of each transformer is 2400 volts,
Line voltage = 1.73 × coil voltage
= 1.73 × 2400
= 4152 volts
If the coil voltage must be checked and the line voltage is known, the same value of 1.73 
can be used. For this situation, the coil voltage is obtained by dividing the line voltage by 1.73.

POLARITY TEST FOR UNMARKED AND NEW 
TRANSFORMERS
The next step is to connect the low-voltage output (secondary) windings in wye (Figure 21–3). 
Follow this procedure when making the secondary connections:
 
1. Check to see that the voltage output of 
each of the three transformers is 120 volts 
(for this example).
  
Caution: Deenergize all circuits before 
making connections.
 
2. Connect the X2 ends of two low-voltage 
secondary windings.
Figure 21–4 illustrates two secondary coils with 
the X2 coil ends connected. The voltage across the 
open ends should be 1.73 × 120 = 208 volts. 
However, if the leads on one transformer are reversed, 
the voltage across the open ends will be 120 volts.
Figure 21–5 illustrates two transformers con-
nected incorrectly. The voltage across the open ends 
is only 120 volts. If the leads of transformer 2 are 
reversed, the connections will be correct, and the volt-
age across the open ends will be 208 volts.
 
3. Connect the X2 lead of the low-voltage 
secondary winding of transformer 3 with 
the X2 leads of the other two transformers.
The proper wye connection of the low-voltage 
secondary windings of the three single-phase trans-

each pair of open ends should be 1.73 × 120 = 
208 volts. If the voltage across the open ends is cor-
rect, then the line leads feeding to the three-phase, 
208-volt secondary system may be connected.

222
Whenever single-phase transformers are connected in wye, the following current and 
voltage relationships are true:
• The line voltage is equal to 1.73 × winding voltage.
• The line current and the winding current are equal.
The wye-wye connection scheme is satisfactory as long as the load on the secondary side 
is generally balanced. The wye-wye connection is unsatisfactory in situations where the second-
ary load becomes greatly unbalanced. An unbalanced load results in a serious unbalance in the 
three output voltages of the transformer bank.
THREE-PHASE, FOUR-WIRE WYE CONNECTION
Voltage unbalancing in the secondary of the transformer bank can be nearly eliminated if a 
fourth wire (neutral wire) is used. This neutral wire connects between the source and the neu-
tral point on the primary side of the transformer bank.
In the connection diagram (Figure 21–8), a three-phase, four-wire system is used to 
feed the three-phase, high-voltage input to the transformer bank. The grounded neutral wire 
is connected to the common point where all three high-voltage primary winding ends or H2 
leads connect. The voltage from the neutral to any one of the three line wires is 2400 volts. 
Each high-voltage winding is connected between the neutral and one of the three line loads. 
Therefore, 2400 volts is applied to each of the three high-voltage primary windings. The volt-
age across the three line leads is 1.73 × 2400 volts, or 4152 volts. The neutral wire maintains a 
relatively constant voltage across each of the high-voltage primary windings, even though the

223
load is unbalanced. Because the neutral wire is grounded, it helps protect the three high-voltage 
primary windings from lightning surges.
A three-phase, four-wire system also feeds from the low-voltage secondary side of the 
transformer bank to the load. Each low-voltage secondary winding is connected between 
the secondary grounded neutral and one of the three line leads. As on the primary side, the 
 grounded neutral helps protect the low-voltage secondary windings from lightning surges.
The voltage output of each secondary winding is 120 volts. The voltage between the neutral 
and any one of the three line leads on the secondary side is 120 volts, as shown in Figure 21–8. The 
voltage across the three line leads is 1.73 × 120 = 208 volts. Thus, by using a three-phase, four-
wire secondary, two voltages are available for different types of loads: 208 volts, three phase, for 
industrial power loads such as three-phase motors, and 120 volts, single phase, for lighting loads.
Many single-phase transformers are designed so that the low-voltage side consists of two 
120-volt windings. These two windings can be connected in series for 240 volts or in parallel 
for 120 volts.
Figure 21–9 shows three single-phase transformers connected as a wye-wye bank. Each 
transformer has two 120-volt, low-voltage windings. For each single-phase transformer, the low-
voltage coils are connected in parallel to give a voltage output of 120 volts. Note in Figure 21–9 
that the secondary output windings of the three transformers are connected in wye. This three-
phase, four-wire secondary system provides two different types of service:
• three-phase, 208-volt service for motor loads
• single-phase, 120-volt service for lighting loads
The 120/208-volt wye system is commonly used in schools, stores, and offices. Another 
popular system for large installations is the 480/277-volt wye system. In many modern instal-
lations, 480-volt systems are desired for large motor loads such as supermarkets, large retail 
stores, manufacturing facilities, and so on. By utilizing a four-wire, wye secondary pattern, the 
voltage on the secondary is 277 volts to ground and 480 volts between lines. Many luminaires 
are designed for a 277-volt, single-phase line to ground, known as a grounded neutral conduc-
tor operation. Some applications of this system include
• motors connected to 480 volts (phase to phase)
• fluorescent luminaires connected to 277 volts (phase to neutral)
• 120-volt outlets, incandescent lamps, and appliances connected to 120-volt circuits sup-
plied from single-phase, 480/120/240-volt transformers or three-phase, 480/208Y/120-
volt transformers. These separate transformers are connected to the 480-volt feeders for 
the primary source.
Three single-phase transformers of the same kilovolt-ampere capacity are used in most 
wye-wye connected transformer banks. The total kilovolt-ampere capacity of a wye-wye 
connected bank is found by adding the individual kVA ratings of the transformer. If each 
transformer is rated at 25 kVA, then the total kVA is 25 + 25 + 25 = 75 kVA.

225
If one transformer becomes defective, it must be replaced before the transformer bank 
can be re-energized. A wye-wye connected transformer bank cannot be reconnected in an 
emergency situation by using only two single-phase transformers, such as in the open-delta 
system. Three-phase capacity can also be calculated by using the line voltages and line current: 
3-phase KVA = Line E × Line I × 1.73.
SUMMARY
Single-phase transformers can be connected in a wye pattern to achieve the desired three-phase 
and single-phase voltages required by many commercial customers. By connecting the single 
phases in a wye pattern, the line voltages can be increased by a factor of 1.73 times the coil volt-
age. This increased level of voltage is often desirable to reduce the line current drawn by a load. 
By increasing the voltage, the current will be less for a specific wattage load. Be sure to check 
all transformer polarities, and check the final connections for a solid ground if using the three-
phase, four-wire wye-connected system.

U N I T  2 2   A LT E R N AT E  W Y E  A N D  D E LTA   C O N N E C T I O N S
